HARDSHIPS OF
GOLD-HUNTERS ON
ARCTIC SHORES
Prospectors on the Porcupine
River Found After Thrilling
Adventures.
Second Party Led by Scott Mason of Chi
cago Depart After Being Rescued.
It Is Feared All Have Perished.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. Ci Aug. 7.â According to arrivals by the
steamer Rosalia. John F. Robertson and a party of eleven
prospectors, who set out from Dawson City a year ago to
prospect along the Porcupine River, returned to the Klon
dike in an emaciated condition on July 22. At Dawson they
told a story of suffering and hardship, a story of narrow escapes
from death time and again in their fruitless search for gold.
They reached Dawson on the steamer Sault Ste. Marie,
which vessel picked them up at the confluence of the Porcupine
and Yukon. The unfortunate prospectors left Dawson because
of a report of a lone miner- who came in from the headwaters
of the Porcupine and told of rich finds there. The Robertson
party, led by the miner, started for the scene of his reported
finds, but during their journey the miner was drowned, and al
though they searched the country thoroughly Robertson and his
party failed to find a trace of the dead man's El Dorado.
Failing to find the golden creeks, they worked their way
across the mountains to the Arctic, intending to prospect en
route, but they found no prospects that induced them to stop.
Colors were found in places, but no pay dirt.
After a trying journey the wandering argonauts at length
reached the Arctic, and were camped on the ocean's shores
near Herschell Island with their provisions all but exhausted
when they were found by a whaler's longboat and by its occu
pants taken to their ship, the only vessel then at anchor at the
Arctic whaling depot. . ..*.._ ,; .. .. ,â.. ; *.~-,. Â .. 'Â£&Â£* -- -.
The Scott Mason ''party* of Chicago had been found on the
Arctic shores by the whalers a short time before the Robert
son party were picked up, and. after being housed and fed for
a month, they had left, intending to make a short prospecting
tour and return. They had not returned, however, and it was
feared they have perished.
The Robertson party spent the winter on the whaler and
then started on the journey back to Dawson. The reminis
cences of their trip are replete with hairbreadth escapes. On
one occasion the raft on which they were drifting down the
Porcupine was swamped, and they had to scramble ashore
leaving their food in the river bed. They managed to subsist,
however, on the game of the northern country, for Robertson
says enormous numbers of cariboo are to be found there.
They were in a very weak condition from the effects of
their journey when the Yukon was reached.

TERRIFIC EXPLOSION
WRECKS GAS PLANT
Works Supplying Wabash, Ind.,
With Illumination Entirely
Destroyed.
WABASH, Ind.. Aug. The gas works
of the Logansport and Wabash Valley
Gals Company were destroyed by a terrific
explosion, which shook the city, at 5
o'clock to-day. Workmen had just com
pleted charging the tanks with calcium
carbide and water, from which gas acety
lene is manufactured, when an explosion
occurred. The company installed the acet
ylene plant less than a year ago. being
the first ever put In for Illuminating a
city with the new light. Scores of plate
glass windows were broken by the shock
in the business part of town.
To-night the city is In darkness and
there will be no light until the new works
are constructed. The loss is heavy.

RENNES, Aug. 7.â At 6:30 a. m. this
pretty city of Rennes, neatly

HARSHNESS TO DISARM
ENEMIES OF DREYFUS
RENNES. Aug. 7.â Mme. Dreyfus visited her husband this after
noon and found him hopeful, although much unnerved, as the rigorous
examination, and especially the unfeeling demeanor displayed toward
him by Colonel Jouaust, proved a tremendous strain upon him.
There are many persons who were present at the trial now under the
impression that the harshness of the presiding judge was assumed and
was intended to disarm the enemies of Dreyfus, who are exceedingly
anxious to interpret ordinary courtesy into the grossest partiality
toward the prisoner. If this be the case, and such an explanation is
quite plausible, Dreyfus is naturally unaware of it, and the effect upon
his highly strung nerves must be very severe.
I the Jews!" or anything like that. Th?
' Paris journalists who had come to re
j port the trial seemed calmed by the
I quietude of this dead city.
It was expected that the Dreyfus
| ites and anti-Dreyfusites would shun
i each other and put up at different
i hotels, but they have tacitly agreed to
I sink their differences in each others
| company. They not only lodge at the
same hotels, but they take their meals
at the same tables and amicably pass
'to each other the dishes. Rennes does
not want them to be theatrical, and so
i they do not pose as ferocious patriots,
: but are glad to drop the characters as
sumed for the drama-loving public of
Paris. tf'tftf '' : ''\ytf
No idea exists in Rennes that the
eyes of the world are upon her, yet
there must be within her walls 300
I men, representing journalism all the
i world over. It never before occurred to

, press isâ or, at least, I never realized
it so peculiarly.

and left of the Lycee door. An offi

close to me and entered a room, the

saw -also the arrival of the witnesses,
who were taken to the rector's room-
Generals Billot, Mercier, Chanoine,

were in uniform, as were many of the

The widow of Colonel Henry leaned
upon the arm of Captain Wunos. She
was most amiably saluted by Generals
de Boisdeffre and Mercier. She looks
stagy and not ladylike. General Pel

Lieutenant Colonel Picquart was in

by the army people. But apparently
ne did not mind. He had played the
winning card and could afford to be

The concert-room, where the trial is
held, has a vaulted roof. The walls are
adorned with tablets containing the
names of famous Bretons. Just be
hind the seat placed for Dreyfus is a
tablet on which the last name is that
of a Roman. '-, â¢>T
At 7 o'clock the coming of the court
was announced in military fashion,
the soldiers presenting arms. The
judges entered from the wings and
were in full dress uniform. They pro
ceeded in Indian file to their places be
hind a long table on a slightly raised
patform. Colonel Jouaust, the presid
ing officer, is a man of dignified ap
pearance, not unintellectual, and has
an upright, martial air. The counsel
for the defense have seats and desks
on the stag- or platform to the left of
the judges' table. The seat of the ac
cused is in front and also on the stage.
Facing it on the right side are desks
for the registrar and prosecuting of
ficer. All were well in view.
Colonel Jouaust in stentorian voice
declared that the trial be begun and
that, as ordered, the accused be
Drought in. All eyes were turned upon
Dreyfus. His fresh complexion aston

ished all. The figure remains clean
Duilt. He entered with measured step.
His mode of saluting the court was
jerky and very composed, but his fin
gers betrayed nervousness.. His voice
is not good, but it was out of practice
for five years.
The first incident was the declaration
of the President Colonel that he had
unlimited power to call witnesses. He
then ordered those of M. Quesnay de
Beaurepaire to be cited. The indict
ment is that of 1894.
Dreyfus answered cautiously all
questions. The Judge was fair but
evidently not friendly. He showed
himself, however, sensible and sober
minded and impressed every one fa
vorably.
The accused is unfortunate in not
being able, apart from his sufferings,
as told by others, to command sym
pathy. His countenance only expressed
a wish to hide his feelings. It is a
reticent face, but not a bad one. One
would like it to be more frank- The
voice is not pleasing, and the diction is
less so.
Dreyfus seems without dramatic feel
ing and lacks ease in all things. How
ever, he passed to-day through a cruel
ordeal. It was trying, after the ex
perience of the last five years, to find
himself in the full blaze of publicity
and stared at and scrutinized by 700
observers.
In the course of a conversation with
Maitre Demange he remarked that
Dreyfus seemed to be unresponsive and
uncapable of emitting a flow of feeling.
"Think," he said, "what I. or you would
be if for five years out of humanity a
reach constantly under the eye of a
surly jailer, and not only under his eye,
but within the aim of his revolver. He
was bound to silence unless when given
leave to speak. He was beset with ene
mies, was treated as the vilest of mor
tals, was put in irons, though docile and
submissive. He almost learned to cow
er. He found he was losing the lingual
faculties and had to speak to himself
to keep jtup.lknew hitnj)gfcre he was
sent to Devils Island. He" was then
hearty, responsive and really good
company with those he His voice
was singularly tuneful and expressive,
but he lost it.
â¢'Dreyfus is now the ghost of his
former self. All spirit was broken by
the tyrannical treatment, the vapor
bath, the noxious insects and the isola
tion from all intelligent human beings.
He is still proud, but he is cowed. That
hollow voice is such evidence to me of
long and indescribable suffering. It is
a far-off voice, like what one hears in a
telephone. His lachrymal glands are
worn out. In short, he is a wreck.
"I wish his pride would suffer him to
collapse. If it did he would draw tears
from the most stony-hearted. He seem
ed to me miraculous as he bore himself
through all thqse long hours, the, center
to which all eyes converged.: Dreyfus,
I repeat, was a a charming man five
years ago. He was thought fascinating
by the other sex. Only; a lovable man
could have been loved as he has been
by his wife." EMILY CRAWFORD.
"I AM INNOCENT!"
DECLARES DREYFUS
Rigid Examination of the
Famous Prisoner by
Colonel Jouaust.
RENNES, Aug. 7.â lnteresting scenes
attended the opening of the second
court-martial of Dreyfus this morning.
At 7 o'clock MM. Laborie and De
mange and Major Carriere, with as
sistants, took their seats and the wit
nesses followed.
MM. Laborie and Demange were
greeted with warm handshakes from
numerous friends in the courtroom.
Former President Casimir-Perier en
tered shortly before 7. An officer met
him at the door and conducted him
to the velvet-covered chairs reserved
for witnesses. The ex-President found
himself between Generals Billot and
Chanoin, both in parade uniform.
Other ex-Ministers of War, Generals
Mercier and Zurlinden and Cavaignac
were seated behind. Colonel Picquart
took a seat without any demonstration
for or against him. '..--.> '-':'"
The officer commanding the row ot
soldiers at the back of the court gave
the command "Carry arms!" "Present
arms!" There was a rattle of arms, and
a moment later Colonel Jouaust, fol
lowed by the other members of the
court, walked on the stage from a room
behind and took seats at the table.
Deep silence fell upon the audience,
who up to then had engaged in a buzz
of conversation. .
The members of the court-martial are
as follows: President, Colonel Jouaust,
director in the Engineer Corps; Lieu
tenant Colonel Brongniart. director of
the School of Artillery; Major de Breon
of the Seventh Regiment of Artillery;
Major Pronllet of the Tenth Regiment
of Artillery; Major Merle of the Sev
enth Regiment of Artillery: Captain
Parfait of the Seventh Regiment of Ar
tillery: Captain Beauvais of the Sev
enth Regiment of Artillery.
Colonel Jouaust and his colleagues
wore full parade uniforms, with aigrettes
in the front of the peaked shakos. On
the right hand of Colonel Jouaust sat
Lieutenant Colonel Brongniart, Major de
Breon and Captain Parfait, all of the ar
tillery. On his left hand were Majors
Profillet and Merle and Captain Beauvais,
also of the artillery.
Immediately after Colonel Jouaust was
seated he gave the order to bring in tne
prisoner. All eyes were then turned to
the right of the stage, on which side was
a door leading Into the room in which
Dreyfus was awaiting the summons. Al
most everybody but the most prominent
officers stood on their feet; some mounted
the benches to qbtain a better view.
There were subdued cries of Sit down,
amid which the door opened and Captain
Alfred Dreyfus, preceded and followed by
a gendarme, emerged into the courtroom.
His features were deathly pale and his
teeth were set with a determined but not
defiant bearing. He walked quietly with
almost an elastic step, and ascended the
three steps leading to the platform in
front of the Judges. There he drew him
self up erect, brought his right hand
sharply to the peak of his military cap,2

cept that he saw in the newspapers that
Mr. Carnegie had been appointed a deputy
lieutenant. He was not aware whether
Mr. Carnegie was naturalized or not and
undoubtedly the Lord Chancellor and
Lord Lieutenant did not know the cir
cumstance when they made .the appoint
ment.
Joseph Chamberlain, Secretary of State
for the Colonies, replying to a question on
the subject, said that as far as the Trans
vaal was concerned he had nothing to add
or to withdraw from the explanation lie
made ten days ago.
In the course of a speech on the situa
tion in Sierra Leone. Mr. Chamberlain'
complimented the work of the mission
aries there, especially the Americans, who
he said were extremely practical men and
always sought to confer direct benefits on
the people. 'â¢â¢'./

missioner had. contracted a serious dis

a colleague whispered a communication to
him.
Mr. Chamberlain immediately an
nounced that he had been greatly shocked
on learning that Sir David Patrick Chal
mers died last Saturday.
DEWEY'S EYE ON MANILA
TWENTY-FIVE YEARS AGO
Requested Permission to Capture the
City at the Time of the
Virginius Affair.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 7.â An interesting
historical fact dating back to .873 has
come to light in which Admiral Dewey
was the centra', figure. Dewey, then a
commander, was in command of the
United States ship Narragansett on the
Astatic station, having taken charge of
the vessel on March l. 1873. The vessel
was on surveying duty when the Vir
ginlus trouble was precipitated and a" war
with Spain seemed imminent. Commander
Dewey wrote to the Navy Department re
questing that in case war was declared he
be assigned to the duty of capturing Ma
nila. The peaceful settlement of the con
troversy with Spain avoided th.- necessity
for a hostile demonstration, but the in
teresting fact Is that the doughty officer
had his eye on Manila over a quarter of
a century ago. â . * ; 'Â.....
A search twill he made for the letter m
the flies of the Navy Department and if it
is found it is expected an effort will be
made by the citizens' committee to have
it reproduced as a souvenir of the recep
tion to be tendered Admiral Dewey on his
arrival here.
LONDON. -Aug. B.â The Rome corre
spondent of the Daily Mail says Admiral
Dewey will arrive there on Wednesday
and that he has asked for an audience
with the Pope. According to the same
correspondent the admiral will visit Lon
don before returning to the United States.
LABOR POLITICAL PARTY
FORMED IN NEW YORK
NEW YORK, Aug. 7.-- response to a
call issued some time ago by the Central
Federated Union twenty-one delegates,
representing 105 labor organizations, met
to-night in Clarendon Hall to consider the
advisability of forming a permanent la
bor political party. President Samuel
Prince of the Central Federated Union
called the organization to order.
The credential committee declined to ad
mit Edward Friday of the Clothing
Salesmen's Union on the ground that he
is a Tammany politician and an inspector
on the Third Avenue Railway and not a
bona fide salesman. William Marlin, with
credentials from the Knights of Labor
local assembly 1563. was rejected on the
ground that he had been a candidate on
the last Republican ticket- - ".'â ;â¢
Joseph Barondoos of the tailors, leader
of a score of strikes, made a passionate
appeal for immediate organization.
The proposition to form a permanent
organization was then submitted to a
vote and the following officers were elect
ed:
Chairman, William J. O'Brien, president
of the board of walking delegates and
Granite Cutters' Union; vice chairman,
T. M. Daly; secretary, Ernest Rohm;
treasurer, Patrick McCarthy; serreant at
arms, Richard Cooney.

CAR WAS RUNNING
AT HIGH SPEED
Cause of the Bridgeport
Horror.
WHEELS LEFT THE RAILS
BOUNDED WHEN THEY STRUCK
THE BRIDGE.
Motorrnan Hamilton Arraigned in
Justice Court for Manslaughter .
and Released on
Bail.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BRIDGEPORT, Conn., Aug. 7.-An open |
trolley car of the Shelton Extension Com
pany, twisted, bent and smashed almost
Into kindling wood, lying In the middle I
of Peck's millpond at Oronoque about six
miles north of here, this morninj*. Â« th ,Â°
only visible indication of the frightful ac- ,
cident of yesterday afternoon, when twen
ty-nine lives were lost and about a dozen
persons seriously injured by the car ,
tumbling off the trestle which crosses the
pond at this point. . j
George Hamilton, the motorrnan of the ,
car. was arraigned in the Stratford Jus- j
tice Court, charged with manslaughter.
He pleaded not guilty and the case ** as
continued for one week. The prise* Â£
bail was fixed at $2000. which he supplied.
The Coroner's jury made a^ thorough
Inspection of the trestle and its ap
proaches to-day. The structure appa
rently is substantial. The "trirawson
the outside of each "-Â«"â¢â¦Â»Â£Â«_____
six by ten inches, bolted to the ties _and
intended to prevent a car leaving the- rails.
The rails, however, measure live ana a
half inches from base to top. thus i being
only one-half inch lower than the wo.m en
stringer on the guard .ail H>'l\Y; s .: l^'
lives of the jury measured I the Â«i -stance
which the car ran after leaving the Irons
as indicated by marks and scars on the
ties, and found It to be eighty-five feet.
At the south end of the bridge is an
earthwork approach 250 feet in length,
with a 3 per cent grade. The bridge ex
perts who to-day accompanied the jur\
said that a car running rapidly over this
earth approach would acquire a springy.
swaying motion because of the elastic
properties of the earth. If a car traveled
down the approach at a high rate or
.peed, they said, it would at the time the
trestle was reached be swaying [consider
ably. So long as it remained on the
earthen foundation there would be little
danger, of It leaving the irons, but when
ii-,. bridge was reached the elasticity of
the foundation immediately would be re
moved, the bridge being rigid, and the ten- â ,
dency would be for the car to spring up j
from the rails. .â¢â¢','â â _, _
The car. which had sunk In the mud
beneath the bridge, was removed -and
carefully examined. The wheel flanges ;
were found to be In perfect condition. -he
brake and brake rod also wore uninjured.
The brakes were set. The condition *Â»i
the lever by which the power was regu
lated showed that at the time the car left
the bridge the power was turned com- i
pletely off.
TWENTY VICTIMS OF
FERRY SLIP ACCIDENT
BAR HARBOR. Me.. Aug. 7.â A careful
examination by divers in the vicinity of
j the Mount Desert ferry slip, the scene of
yesterday's accident, and investigation oh
shore seem to Indicate that the twenty
victims reported last night are all who
perished as a result of the catastrophe.
Mr. Southard of Bangor, who was seri
ously injured, is not likely to recover, and
two other persons are suffering from
pneumonia. The others injured are in a
favorable condition.
The* complete list of dead ' show? no
change from that sent out last night, save
| that the woman who was designated as
unknown has been identified as Mrs.
Mower of California.
PUBLIC AUTOMOBILE
SERVICE IN NEW JERSEY
Syndicate Formed to Run Vehicles
Between Philadelphia and
New York.
TRENTON. Aug. 7.â This city and all
the larger cities of New Jersey will soon
have a public service of automobiles. To
provide this the New Jersey Electric Ve
hicle and Transportation Company has
increased its capital from $100,000 to $1,200,
--000, a certificate to that effect being filed
to-day. This company is backed by the
capital of the Whitney-ETkins syndicate,
of which James E. Hayes, president of
the company and formerly law partner of
the late George M. Robeson. Secretary of
the Navy under General Grant, is the
New Jersey representative. .
Mr. Hayes said to-night that an auto
mobile service would at once be estab
lished in the larger cities and towns of
the State and at the coast resorts.
"We will establish a connecting system
between New York and Philadelphia," he
said, "and expect to make the New Jer
sey company our working model for other
States. We have formed twenty com
panies under the Now Jersey laws and
expect to make this the center of our en
tiro system. On our conveyances in this
State will be put all the modern appli
ances.
"For the present our headquarters in
this State will be Jersey City, although
it is probable we will soon establish
branches at Trenton to have supervision
over the southern end of the State. Al
ready we have 4500 vehicles constructed
and we will begin the operation of our
system at once."

SOLDIERS CHEER THE
CLEVELAND STRIKERS
j CLEVELAND, Ohio, Aug. More per
i sons rode on the cars of the Big Consoli
! dated Streetcar Company to-day than on
! any day since the boycott was declared.
A new feature of the strike was the act
! of prominent business men in -signing, a
call for a meeting to be held in the Cham
! ber of Commerce building to-morrow
night to organize a law and order league
to fight the boycott. Sympathy for the
strikers has extended to the soldiers who
are' guarding the property of the street
car company, and to-day. when a com
pany of the Fourth Regiment lined up at
the Miles-avenue barns In Newburg. the
soldiers gave three cheers for the strik
ers. These were followed by three lusty
groans for the non-union men.
To-day the local Brotherhood of loco
motive Engineers' lodge sent a Contribu
tion of $mo and a letter to the*, strikers.
In the letter the lodge said the contribu
tion was made because P. M. Arthur.
chief of the brotherhood, had advanced
himself in the favor of the company and
had ridden on the boycotted cars. The
lodge by a unanimous vote censured the
chief.
To-day the newsboys declared a boycott
on the Leader and Plain Dealer, morning
newspapers, because of their favorable
attitude toward the streetcar company.

NEW REGIMENTS
TO BE HURRIED TO
THE PHILIPPINES
Otis Will Be Reinforced for the
Fall Campaign by the First
of November.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, WELLING
TON HOTEL, WASHINGTON, Aug. 7.â
In accordance with the new policy inaug
urated by Secretary Root to expedite the
departure of reinforcements for General
Otis. orders were telegraphed to recruit
ing officers to-day by Colonel Thomas
Ward, assistant adjutant general, direct
ing them to complete the enlistment of
men for new volunteer regiments by the
end. of the present week.
Th regiments will then be carefully
drilled. at" their present camps and will,
department officials say, be prepared to
commence the journey to Manila by-
September 1. Quartermaster General
Luddington told me this afternoon that
the transports Sherman and Grant would
be at San Francisco the latter part of the
month, and they will be able to accommo
date two of the regiments. Other ships
will be at San Francisco early in Septem
ber. The Logan and the Thomas, which are
being converted into modern troopships
at New York and Philadelphia, will be
completed as rapidly as possible, and they
undoubtedly will be ready in time to con
vey two of the volunteer regiments to the
Philippines. -..-,'
By the early part of November all the
reinforcements now under orders to join
General Otis will, it is stated, have
reached their destination, and he will
have 46,000 men under his command to
engage in the fall campaign. '*._ ' .
Of the 13,000 men required for the ten
regiments being organized in this country
Colonel Ward told me to-day that 10,3.52
have been enlisted, leaving 275S yet to be
mustered into service. As enlistments
have been made in the past at the rate of
510 per day no doubt is expressed that the
department's orders sent put to-day <Â»-'
reeling that the regiments be h led by toe
end of the week will be complied with
The Twenty-seventh and lhirty-nrst
regiments now have more men than they
were allowed by the President s orders,
but over-enlistments were permitted in
order that they might work out some of
the undesirable men who have undoubt
edly entered the service.
Probably because of the sentiment ex
isting in the South against the war in the
Philippines the War Department has
found that it will be impossible to Ml the
two regiments being organized at Fort
McPherson, Georgia, and Fort Sam
Houston with Southern recruits. The re
cruiting officers at Fort McPherson have
succeeded in enlisting only 635 men, while
those at Sam Houston have obtained but
545 men. Orders will, therefore, be issued
directing that Northern and Western men
be drafted into these regiments until UV'ir
entire complement is obtained. The de
partment has not obtained a more satis
factory reason for the unwillingness of
Southerners to enter the army than the
sentiment which appears to exist against
continuing the struggle to suppress the
rebellion. There is reason to believe that
Major General Miles is in favor of increas- j
ing the cavalry force in the Philippines to
10,000 men, and as the Herald has stated
the department is considering the art visa- ,
bility of sending more cavalry to Manila. |
General Otis has reported that he Is ob- |
taining a number of men for the volun
teer cavalry regiment he is organizing at i
Manila, but the department Is making ar
rangements to ship cavalry recruits along |
STRIFE OVER A PIANO
MAY END IN COURT
SAN* RAFAEL, Aug. 7â The sensation
at Bollnas over the arrest of three prom
inent society persons on the charge of
house-breaking, it being alleged that they
entered the house of William MeGovern
and took therefrom a piano, is rivaled in
local school circles. Miss Jessie Elliott,
the teacher of the San Rafael Kinder
garten, now has a piano which she had
carted away from the rooms of the Moth
ers' Club without the consent of the own
ers, and it is probable that her arrest will
follow.
Four years ago a social and temperance
organization existed in this city known
as "The Y's." The members held enter
tainments and with the money thus ob
tained purchased a good piano. The club
finally disbanded and at Miss Elliott f s
expressed desire placed the instrument in
her hands tor safe keeping. For the use.
of it she agreed to keep It in order, and
promised to surrender it when a majority
of the members so wished. ~ tftf
Three weeks ago the members of the
disbanded club were asked to permit the
Mothers' Club to use the piano at a cer
tain monthly rental, and concluded so to
do. William McKenzie, the secretary of
"The V's," was sent to Miss Elliott for
the piano. She refused at first to sur
render it, but finally became angry and
said that if it was not taken out of her
house by 9 o'clock the next morning she
would take a hatchet and destroy it. An
expressman was sent for the instrument
and removed it to the apartments of the
Mothers* Club in the High School build-
Ing.
Three days afterward Miss Elliott sent
another expressman to the High School
building while the janitor was engaged
in cleaning the halls and had the piano
brought back to her house. Since then
she has refused to relinquish possession
of it. McKenzie threatened her with ar
rest, but she remained obdurate. The
High School opened to-day and the
Mothers' Club has to carry on Its work
without the consoling strains of the
piano.
Secretary McKenzie was suddenly called
to Bolinas and said before he departed
that on his return he intended to have
Miss Elliott placed under arrest. The
Mothers' Club includes in its membership
the wives of some of the most prominent
citizens of the community. Miss Elliott
is the best known of local kindergarten
teachers.
HOSPITAL ATTENDANTS
CHARGED WITH MURDER
NEW YORK, Aug. 7.â An examination
of the body of James McGuire, formerly
a paretic inmate at the Manhattan Hos
pital on Wards Island, and who died in
that institution on Saturday, led to the
arrest to-night of four keepers, Thomas
Sexton. Patrick Ryan, Daniel O'Connell
and Martin White, who may have to
answer to a charge of murder. The ar
rests were made on the strength of the
report of the Coroner's physician who
conducted the post-mortem and reached
the conclusion that McGuire met death
by violence.
Scarcely a spot en the dead man's body
was free from bruises, which apparently
were the result, not of blows from a club
or other instrument, but of pressure from
the knees of the attendants. From his
head to his knees deep blue dents were
discernible on the flesh. Six of the ribs
on the right side were* broken. The In
testines had broken through the abdom
inal membrane, while the membrane itself
had been torn almost to ribbons. The
kidneys and liver had been badly bruised
and although there Was no fracture of
the skull there was a large abrasion at
the back of the head. - . -- .*.-.-
McGuire. before becoming insane, had
been a professional strong man and
wrestler.
PROPOSE TO LICENSE â¢
PUBLIC UTILITIES
HOLLISTER, Aug. 7.â The District At
torney has been instructed to prepare a
license ordinance and in future all tele
phone, telegraph and express companies
will be required to procure licenses to
transact business in San Benito County.
The action of Wells, Fargo & Co. in
requiring patrons to pay the War tax has
aroused the greatest hostility to the com
pany, and a vigorous effort will be made
by town and county to bring It to book.
At the session of the Town ' Trustees
to-night a license ordinance affecting
telephone,, telegraph and express com
panies was passed. The officials here are
in earnest and have ample funds to carry
the case through to the highest courts.

SHIELDS' PRAISE OF
A SLAIN ORDERLY
WASHINGTON, Aug. Major George
F. Shields, surgeon of the Third Brigade
at Manila, has made a report to Surgeon
General Sternberg under date of June 1
of his duties during April and May. In
it he specially commends Private Paul
Gompers of the Hospital Corps, who was
killed in action April 25. â¢ ''< '-":-'â â 'â¢ â >
Major Shields says: "On April 25. dur
ing the advance at the river Bagbag, my
orderly. Private Paul Gompers, was fa
tally wounded while on the armored car,
a Remington bullet passing through his
neck, completely severing the carotid ar
tery and jugular vein on both sides. I
beg here to call the attention of the sur
geon general to the services of this man.
He was constantly with me from Feb
ruary 5 until the day of his death. "We
were' together on the firing line during
the battle and after the battle, and he
performed his duty so bravely, so faith
fully and so unselfishly that he gained
the respect and admiration of the com
manding general and his staff, as well as
all with whom he came in contact. 1
could quote instance after instance of
his bravery, but I feel that the collective
statements*- which I have made will be
evidence enough of the fact that he was
a man of unusual merit."
GUNBOAT NAPIDAN
SHELLS PAETE
MANILA. Aug. 2 (via Hongkong).â The
gunboat Napldan last week shelled Paete
on the lake near Santa Cruz. The town
was full of people who had been en
couraged to return after General Law
ton's expedition, having been assured that
they would not be molested if they peace
ably attended to their business.
Lieutenant Copp, who was in command
of the Napidan, heard that the in
surgents had reoccupied the town and
steaming close in opened fire with his
six-pounders without warning.
The people, seeing the boat approaching,
fled to the hills in a terrified condition and
with barely time to. escape. One child
was killed and many buildings were dam
aged. The authorities express great re
gret on account of the incident.
After the taking of Calamha by the
Americans General Lawton ordered that
Captain Otis of the Washington regiment
be relieved of his command and placed
under arrest on account of slowness and
seeming reluctance of the companies un
der his command in obeying the order to
disembarked from the cascoes and wade
through the marshes under fire. The men
say that a majority of them have been
sick and unfit for duty and were given
to understand that they wouldn't be
asked to do any more fighting.

morning. He has not regained conscious-*
ness'since the attack, and it is feared ho
cannot recover. Dr. Fay is a Uni
tarian minister. He formerly held
pastorates in Boston and Los Angeles.
DEATH OF JUDGE FROST.
Woodland Jurist Passes Away at
Santa Rosa.
SANTA ROSA, Aug. -7.â Judge _. 'â¢' S.
Frost of Woodland died at the home of his
daughter, Mrs. Reindollar, in this city
this afternoon. Judge Frost came to this
State in 1549. In the early days he en

-1 FARE bOoLIGHTHOUSE
AT BALABAC
CAPTURED
Manilo, Chief of the Molos.
Killed and Seven Pris
oners Taken.
NO AMERICAN LOSS
Party Now Guarding the Light
"Will Be Reinforced by Men
From the Charleston.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON. Aug. 7.â The follow
ing dispatch has been received from
Admiral Watson, in command of the
Asiatic station:
Secretary of the Navy, "Washington:
Nazro, the commander of the Manila, on
July 7 .re-established the lighthouse at
("ape Melville, Balabac, Philippine Islands,
and hoisted the nag with appropriate
honors. July 13, on his return, thirteen
Moros. under arms, were found to be in
possession of the lighthouse. Landing
party, commanded by Ensign E. L. Bis
sett. encountered resistance. There were
no casualties In our party. The enemy
lost one officer and one "man. Diegagb
Manilo, chief of the Balabac Moros, was
killed, seven were taken prisoners and
two escaped. The chief of the party was
very unpopular with the people of Bala
bac owing to fear of him. The lighthouse
has been restored. The Charleston Is on
her way to Balabac and will rend all
assistance possible. WATSON.
The Island of Balabac, the scene of
the brush with the Moros, detailed in
Admiral Watson's dispatch, is situated
poutheast of the island of Palawan,
which is the most westerly of the Phil
ippine Islands and directly west of the
island of Pa nay. The light there, which
was re-established by "Lieutenant Com
mander Nazro, shows for twenty-five
miles and is regarded as of consider
able importance to navigation, being
directly in the line between Singapore
and the Straits Settlements and the isl
ands to he north. The island is twen
ty-two miles long and. according to in
formation in the possession of the de
partment, has but a population of 1100.
These are described in Admiral Wat
son's dispatch as Moros, the name ap
plied to the fierce natives of the Sulu
group. They are Mohammedans and
nre regarded as the most warlike peo
ple in the Philippines. The capital.
Balabac, has a population of 200. The
Spp.nish Government established a mil
itary post there in 1857. It consists
of a barracks, a hospital and an armed
fort.
The Manila, the vessel described in
the dispatch, is a captured transport.
Its mission in that vicinity is unknown
at the Navy Department. It is evident
from the dispatch that the Manila has
left a landing force there to guard the
light and that, the Charleston, which
is on its way there, will see that the
landing party receives such reinforce
ments as may be deemed necessary.

CANADA COMPLAINS.
Seizure of Fishing Vessels Leads to
Complications.
PORT TOWNSEND, Aug. 7.â The recent
seizure of several Canadian fishing vessels
by the revenue launch Guard in the vicin
ity of Point Roberts and Blame for fish
ing In American waters has raised some
intricate points In International law. It
is claimed there is no law which prohibits
foreign vessels from fishing in American
waters, and under this claim the British
embassy at Washington made a demand
on the "United States Government for the
immediate release of the vessels. - The
case was referred here for investigation
and the revenue officers say the vessels
were seized for landing nets in American
waters without complying with the cus
toms laws by entering their certificates.
This explanation was not satisfactory,
and advices by wire from the Treasury
Department at Washington say that
United States Consul. Smith at Victor'
has been ordered to proceed to Point Rob
erts and Blame immediately to make a
thorough Investigation and report with
out delay to the department.
FEVER AT HAMPTON.
One New Case Reported, hut No
Deaths.
NEWPORT NEWS, Aug. 7.â There was
one new case of yellow fever at the Na
tional Soldiers* Home to-day, but no
deaths. The case found to-day is the first
reported since last Thursday night. "There
is now no doubt that the backbone of the
epidemic is broken and ln two weeks. It is
probable that the scourge will be a thing
of the past. The quarantine, however,
will' be raised before that time.
A house to house canvass of Phoebus by
Surgeon White and his assistants devel
oped no new cases under suspicion there.

HER HEAD SPLIT
WITH A HATCHET
Mysterious Assault on
Mrs. Kronmann.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
XEW YORK, Aug. 7.â Mrs. Annie Kron
mann was found by her husband. Nathan
Kronmann, in her home on Eighth ave
nue to-night unconscious with two five-
inch gashes in the back of her head.
Beside her on the floor lay a bloody
hatchet. The flat was In perfect order
and Mrs. Kronmann's jewelry, valued at
several thousand dollars, had not been
disturbed. There was no evidence of a
struggle. The woman had evidently been
struck down while passing through the
private hallway. Mrs. Kronmann was
removed to the hospital and up to a late
hour had not regained consciousness. Mr.
Kronmann is a wealthy fruit commission
merchant.
Kronmann was permitted to visit Roose- ;
velt Hospital to-night- Afterward he was
i taken to the West Thirty-seventh street
; station and charged with attempting to ,
murder his wife.
The police say they have learned that a i
woman had been much in Kronmann's ,
company of late, and that she called fre- ;
: quently at his place of business. Kron
: mann and the woman, the police say,
i would frequently spend several hours
I in each other's company, and on such
' occasions he always arrived home late.
When his wife demanded explanations he
â  pleaded business engagements.
Kronrrfann was seen to leave his place
of business accompanied by the woman,
j and he admits that he did not reach his
home until 7 o'clock. When examined at
the police station his coat sleeves and ;
cuffs were found to be stained with
blood, but he says he received the stains
! while lifting his wife out of the hallway.
STATE FAIR COMMITTEES
REPORT FINE PROGRESS
Every Inch of Floor Space in the Big
Exposition Building
Engaged.
SACRAMENTO, Aug. The Citizens' !
State Fair Club met to-night and con- \
sidered various matters in connection !
with the approaching State Fair. E. B.
Willis, managing editor of the Record-
Union, reported that the executive com-
mittee of the California Press Association
had resolved to change Its convention
place from Tallae. Dake Tahoe, to Sac
ramento and 150 editors of the State,
most of them accompanied by their wives,
would enjoy Thursday, _â¢ rlday and Sat
urday of the first week of the State Fair.
Captain H. I. Seymour, chairman of the
committee on local exhibits, reported that
every inch of the lower floor of the vast
exposition building had been engaged and
that until the new exhibitors could In
duce the others to contract their space
an overflow annex would have to be pro
vided.
The Commercial Travelers, the For
esters, the Caledonians, the -Native Sons
and other fraternal and social organiza
tions are each to have a day. at the fair,
and they are already arranging pro
grammes and sending out circulars for
the events.
It is worthy of note that the local Chi
nese and Japanese colonies have become
Interested in the exposition. The Japan
ese are to p.esent some clever athletes
and will show how their native wed&ings
are performed. The Chinese, on the other
hand, will lend the services of a native
orchestra, whose shrill melodies have
often floated across the placid depths of
China Slough. Altogether there is every
promise that the State Fair, which opens
in this city September 4, will be one of
the most attractive ever held in Sacra
mento.

PEACE COMMISSION IS
ACCOMPLISHING MUCH
Optimistic Letter From President Whi
to Dr. Jordan of Stanford
University.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Aug. President Jordan of Stanford
University is in receipt of a private letter from Dr. Andrew D. White,
United States Embassador to Germany and a member of the Interna
tional Peace Commission. The letter Is dated at The Hague. Dr. White
expresses great satisfaction at the outcome of the Peace Convention,
regarding the actual results as much more important than the apparent
ones. The convention must, in his opinion, necessarily open the way for
others of a similar nature and the final result will be the amelioration
of war. the doing away with costly and mischievous armaments and
the ultimate recognition of the principle of arbitration, Which will mean
the establishment of justice, not merely an attempt to reconcile â¢ oppos
ing claims by splitting differences. lÂ».
President White hopes that the American press will realize the situ
ation and will strengthen the work of the convention, not belittle it be
cause it has not at its first session accomplished the impossible nor
done everything that needs doing. It has accomplished all that the
American representatives hoped for and more than they had a right to
expect.

celebration commemorative of the fiftieth
anniversary of the establishment of the
State government of California will be
held here in December. Steps looking to
this end were taken by the City Council
to-day, and Mayor Martin was given
power to appoint a suitable committee of
citizens to take charge of and arrange for
the event. .
The flrst State government was organ
ized here on December 13. 1849, and as this
city was the first capital of California it
1 is Suitable that the honor of holding the
i golden jubilee should some to San Jose, j
1 the State's birthplace. /v r T j
A jubilee committee will be appointed at
' once and plans agreed upon for the cele- j
! bration. It is proposed to make it one
of the greatest events ever held here.
< It is suggested that the affair be in the |
' nature of a fiesta, and that Spanish sports
! and early California games be presented. i
j thus depicting California's infancy.

SUDDEN DEATH
OF DR. JESSEN
Medical Expert Stricken
Yesterday.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA BARBARA, Aug. 7â Dr. H. C. \
Jessen, an authority on materia medica !
and a musical composer of .note, was j
found dead this morning in a hallway I
leading to his office in the Hawley build
ing. Death was due to heart failure.
Three or four days ago Dr. Jessen told
several of his close friends that he ex
pected to die very soon and that he was
"going to pass away in the same mariner
as did Robert G. Ingersoll. " To T. S. Haw
ley he gave the addresses of his two
daughters and son and said that he had
written to them, but that he wished Mr.
Hawley to telegraph them in case any
thing should happen to him before these
letters reached their destination. He also
went to the bank and left his will and
adjusted his affairs. He left a moderately
large estate and money in bank.
Dr. Jessen was the author of several
medical works, among which are "Thera
peutical Materia Medica." "Eczema, Its
Pathology and Treatment," and "The
Pathology and Treatment of Hereditary
Syphills.*" all of which are high authori
ties, and several are used as text books
In the larger Eastern colleges. He was a
member of the Clinical Society of Hanne
mann Hospital of Chicago and of the Hah
nemann Publishing Society of England
and an honorary member of the Instituto
Homeopathico Mexicano of the empire of
Mexico. ... ;. .,-.,
Dr. Jessen was a thorough master of the
violin and author of several musical com
positions. He was 74 years old and a
native of Denmark. His son is now Sur
veyor General of the State of Utah.
APPLY FOR HILLARY
"STARR'S LIFE INSURANCE
Relatives of the Missing Man Are of
the Belief That He Is
Dead.
PASADENA. Aug. 7.â Mrs. Hillary S.
Starr has made an application to the
Grand Lodge of the Maccabees, in behalf
of the family, that it pay the claimants
$2000 to cover the insurance that Starr
carried in that organizafion. From the
moment when on the night of April 24
last. Starr, the foreman of the Oak
Knoli ranch, disappeared, until the pres
ent day no tidings have come from the
absent man. The family in making this
demand pursues the theory that Starr is
dead.
To support the claim S. R. Smith man
ager of the Oak Knoll ranch, makes an
affidavit in which he recites the incidents
of the supposed kidnaping, and particu
larizes the footprints, the broken watch
guard, evidences of the struggle and the
mysterious buggy tracks, in an endeavor
to prove that Starr met foul play at the
hands of persons as yet unknown. Sher
iff Hammill of Los Angeles County and
City Marshal Lacey of Pasadena have
prepared statements that their efforts to
find Starr have been unavailing.
Officers of the Maccabees contend that
litigation may follow, and that payment
may for a long time be deferred unless
more convincing proof of death be pro
duced.
Venezuelan Boundary.
PARIS, Aug. 7.â the session of the
Anglo-Venezuelan boundary arbitration
commission to-day M. Mallet-Prevost,
secretary of the commission, in continuing
his presentation of the Venezuelan case,
discussed various claims relative to the
interior of Guiana, quoting documents to
show that the Dutch never went above
the first cataracts of the Essequibo and
other rivers.

PROSPECTORS
FLEE FROM
THE YAQUIS
Party of Eleven Americans
Reaches Casa Grande
in Safety.
CAUSE OF OUTBREAK
Young Members of the Tribe Dis
satisfied With the Cession of
Mineral Lands and Rights.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CAS A GRANDE, State of Chihua
hua, Mex., Aug. 7. â Eleven more Amer
ican prospectors who abandoned their
placer gold property and fled from the
Yaqui River country to escape being
killed by the Yaqui Indians have ar
rived here. They came in two parties
from the gold fields near the junction of
the Batepit and Paphigochic rivers.
These streams are upper tributaries of
the Yaqui River and are in the very
heart of the old territory inhabited by
the Yaqui Indians before they signed
the treaty of peace with the Mexican
Government over two years ago.
Captain R. Martin Ross, who is well
known in mining circles of Western
Mexico and Arizona, is a member of one
of the prospecting parties that have
just reached here. He was one of the
flrst Americans to enter the unknown
region two years ago. Previous to that
time he had lived for several years in
Ures, Mexico, and has had a long and
Intimate acquaintance with the Yaquis.
He says that he has been expecting the
outbreak to occur for several months
past. The Indians have never been sat
isfied with the surrender of their lands
and mineral rights, which was made
through the influence of the old men of
the tribe. There is an old tradition
among the Yaqui braves to the effect
that they will Increase in numbers and
power until they rule all the lands of
the setting sun. For the past two cen
turies they have made every effort to
resist allegiance to any outside govern
ment, and it did not require much agi
tation to induce the present revolution
against the Mexican Government.
In reference to the danger of Amer
ican prospectors in the Yaqui country.
Captain Ross states that they had the
first warning of the outbreak and the
most of them will have an opportunity
to escape. He estimates that there are
not over 500 American mining men in
the turbulent territory. Several thou
sand prospectors went into the country
from the United States when it was
first opened, but the majority of them
met with disappointment in their search
for gold and did not remain long.
Captain Ross also reports that the
Yaqui braves are in two divisions. One
division is located in the Suhua
rlpa Mountains and the other in
the Bacatete Mountains. Each of these
main forces numbers about 2000 men,
and in addition there are numerous
small bands of roving warriors who are
committing most of the murders and
outrages in the valley.
Another regiment of Mexican cavalry
has just passed through here on its way
to the northern part of the Yaqui coun
try. It is understood this regiment will
be used to drive the force of Indians out
of the Suhuaripa Mountains. A large
force of troops are moving up the valley
from the south.
MILLS SELLS PART
OF HIS OIL LANDS
Standard Company Purchases a Part
of the Farm Near
Corsicana.
AUSTIN, Aug. 7.â Ex-United States
Senator Roger Q. Mills has just sold a
part of his oil land farm near Corsicana
to the Standard Oil Company for $342,000.
There are a number of big producing oil
wells included in the purchase. Senator
Mills retains a large portion of a farm
on which are located many oil wells that
are bringing him in a princely revenue
daily. This farm was worth but a few
thousand dollars before oil was struck
near Corsicana less than two years ago.

SILVER TROPHIES FOR RIVAL
STATE BOYS IN BLUE TO WIN
Utah and Pennsylvania to Meet on the Diamond.
WHEN the Tenth Pennsylvania Volunteers and the Utah Light Ar
tillery returned from the Philippiw a with splendid records won in the
field the newspapers throughout the country extolled their deeds
and published to the world their splendid achievements. Two victo
ries were won by the commands which were omitted in the pub
lished accounts, but to the members of the regiment and battery as much
pride is taken in describing the battles as any in which they participated.
Marry trophies were carried away from Manila, but none more beautiful and
more Interesting than the two silver cups wen by the representatives of the
-eminent and battery by prowess on the diamond. During the prolonged stay
of the volunteer regiments In the Philippines time hung heavily on the hands
of the soldiers. To relieve the monotony the American Commercial Com] of
Manila offered two cups to be contested for by nines from the commands
ihen stationed In the city. The incentive started the men practicing and
within a short time nine men were chosen in m each regiment to uphold the
honor of their organisations. The series of games played were watched by
thousands of soldiers and natives and among the former caused intense ex
citement and enthusiasm. The Pennsylvania*** succeeded in capturing the
first series and were awarded the cup. In the second series the victors did
not participate and Utah's representatives carried off the trophy.
It was the intention of the victors to play for the cups and the champion
ship of Manila, but orders to return to the United States prevented the con
test taking place. The challenge was renewed a few days ago and on Sun
day next the two nines will battle for supremacy on the baseball field at the
Presidio. Both aggregations are assiduously practicing and the game prom
ices to be extremely lively. Charley Newman of the Richelieu, in whose place
the- cups are now on exhibition, has furnished the teams with baseballs and
bats and also promises to furnish refreshments ty the men during the game
and a big dinner to the victorious nine.
It is probable thai Utah will again send forth the nine men who.- did so
we il on the Manila diamond. The team is composed of W. F. Griffiths (cap
tain! H. Klenke, K. A. Walgast, VV. H. Seaver, T. S. Gunn, N. < '. Margetts,
Lieutenant K. C. Naylor, XV. H. Shearer, E. H. Roberts Jr. Lindsay Hudson.
manager of the aggregation, is confident of victory. The Pennsylvanians have
not as .',,.. decided on their line-up. They are anxious to add another trophy
to their* collection and intend putting forth the best talent to do it.

GREAT FLEET OF YACHTS.
New York Club's Craft Anchored Off
New London.
NEW LONDON, Conn., Aug The
great fleet of the New York Yacht Club
lies off the Pequol House to-night, and
will start on its annual cruise to-mor
row. One hundred and throe captains re
sponded when the names of their yachts
were called on board the flagship Corsair
to-day. Probably fifty or sixty additional
vessels are riding in the waters of the
Thames.
The yachts are strung in a line eight or
ten deep from the New London, light
nearly to Fort Grlswold, presenting a
magnificent marine picture.. In the cen

SHAMROCK TO BE TOWED
ONLY DURING CALMS
New York Yacht Club Explains the
Concession Made to Sir
Thomas Lipton.
NEW LONDON, Conn., Aug. 7.â The
New York Yacht Club, learning that the
Shamrock was being towed, to-night is
sued the following statement:
In relation to the statement that the Sham
rock had been reported in tow of a steamer in
lisht order off Tuska, and as such action ls re
garded in violation of the deed of the gift and
the conditions governing the match for the
America's cud, the officers of the New York
Yacht Club desire to state that some time
since a communication was received from a
representative of Sir Thomas Linton announc
ing the intention of the latter to take a south
erly course for the Shamrock, and asking
whether in view of the probability of encoun
tering calms on the proposed route it would be
deemed a violation of the match if the Sham
rock availed herself of the presence of a steam
yacht to bo towed during the calm spells.
In view of the fact of the entire good faith
In which the request was made and that the
towing would subject the vessel to greater
strain than the sailing and could therefore not
be made a pretext for evading th? conditions of
the deed of gift that the challenger should
proceed on her own bottom to the port of con
test, the officers of the club sent an answer to
the effect that the New York Yacht Club would
take no exception to the Shamrock being towed
during calm Intervals between breezes. Its
action in consenting not to object to such a
course should not be regarded, however, as a
precedent, and Is applicable only to this par
ticular case.
BUFFALO HARNESS RACES.
BUFFALO. N. V., Aug. 7.â Two stakes
were decided at the opening of the Buffalo
Driving Club's grand circuit meeting at
the Fort Erie track this afternoon, before
about 2500 people. The prettiest work of
the day was done by Fred Kohl in tha
trotting stake for 2:13 performers. Kohl
sold but third choice to Charley Herr and
Tudor Chimes in the pools, but Dickerson
kept him on his feet in good shape, and
he reeled off three heats handily.
Billy Andrews was always favorite for
the pacing stake, and experienced no
trouble in lancing the prize.
The opening. race, the 2:26 trot, was the
contest of the day. Derby Lass and Volo
were the favorites and Tessie S was an
outsider. After Tessie had been nipped at
the wire in the third heat Starr laid her
up three heats. He won the sixth by
several lengths, but the failure of tHe
others to try harder aroused disapproval
in the judges' stand. Results.

Streams Stocked "With Trout.
MAYFIELD, Aug. 7.â Ten thousand
young rainbow trout arrived here this
morning from the California State Fish
Commission. These trout, which toe from
the Sis-sons hatchery*, are to be distrib
uted under the direction of William
Brownell, the local deputy sheriff, among
the adjacent creeks. Five thousand will
be placed in the headwaters of Pescadero
Creek and the remaining 5000 will be di
vided equally between San Antonio or
"Adobe" Creek and San Francisuneto
Creek. The fish in the latter creek will
be placed about six miles from its mouth,
near the place of Judge Allen, and the
"Adobe" Creek fishes will be placed. about
the same distance from the bay. A con
signment of black bass is expected: They
will be placed in a small lake- on the top
o{ Black Mountain.

LAWIOR AND TURNER DRAW.
Honors Even in Their Twenty-Round
Go at Stockton.
STOCKTON. Aug. 7.â The first fights
of the newly incorporated San Joaquin
Club, composed of sporting men of Stock
ton, took place to-night and Avon The
ater was crowded with interested specta
tow*. The first mill was between Young
Peter Jackson and Charlie Turner of
Stockton, once a clever pugilist but now
stale. Jackson was the better man and
in the second round Turner was counted
out to save him from punishment.
The event of the evening was a 20-round
go between Jimmie Lawlor of San Fran
cisco and Rube Turner of Stockton, both
weighing 134 pounds. It was a pretty
sparring match, though rather tame for
the spectators, who wanted- knockout
blows. Both men fought cautiously. I.aw
lor led the oftener. but Turner countered
best. In the twentieth round the fight was
very even and declared a draw, the purse
of *.*"00 being divided. The decision of
Charley Oliver, the referee, was received
with satisfaction. 'â *

THIS INCLUDES YOU! Are you tired of bossism? It is in your power
to suppress it. Have you grown weary of corruption in public affairs? It
rests with you whether such a condition shall continue unchecked. Do you
want an honest administration of. the municipal government? Co-operation
on your part will secure it.
ATTEND THE PRIMARY; CAST YOUR BALLOT EARLY: CONSIDER
THE ELECTION OF AUGUST 8 AS OF EQUAL IF NOT GREATER IM-
PORTANCE THAN THAT OF NOVEMBER. Your wife and your children
call for this performance of duty by you; your home and your business
demand the protection your ballot alone can give them. Elect men to the
nominating convention who are free and untrammeled, men who will go
there unpledged and free to act accord ing to the best dictates of their con-
science. Consider these names, their weight and standing in the community

Native Sons and Citizens' Commit
tees Will Confer for That
Purpose.
PREPARATIONS for the recep
tion of the California volunteers
went on apace yesterday. Half
a dozen meetings of as many
different committees were held and
much work accomplished.
As a result the decoration commit
tee was allowed $6000 to start with
for the illumination of the city, with
a promise of more if possible and it
should be necessary. That commit
tee is now having necessary plans
and specifications drawn.
-Arrangements were made for the
formal dedication this evening of the
headquarters tent in Union Square,
which will be in charge of the Red
Cross. Mayor Phelan will deliver the
dedicatory speech and Archbishop
Riordan. Father Doherty, Rev. Will
i**m Rider -nd Rabbi Voorsanger
have been invited to make addresses.
Tne executive council of the financa j
committee, in addition to appointing
its full list of collectors, arranged for
a mass-meeting in Chinatown Friday
night raid announced the receipt of
the following sums:

and ire assured of success at the auc- I

the Orpheum.
The Native Sons' committee is
busy arranging for places in the line ;
cf parade for the many societies that
have signified a desire to march.
PARADE plans of the Native Sons
and Daughters on ' reception ' day
may be so changed as to allow of a
purely military procession during the
t afternoon and a grand civic and mili
tary parade at night under the canopy of
incandescent lamps which it is the pur
pose of the citizens' decoration- commit
tee to put in place..
This idea, suggested earlier in the day.
was laid before the Native Sons' and
Daughters' parade' committee last night
by W. J. Martin of the citizens' executive
committee. He said that the citizens' dec
oration committee would have $6000 for its
purposes and would in- pleased to have
the Native Sons and Daughters act with
it to secure the best results for the ex
penditure.
Colonel W. P. Sullivan Jr.. representing
th( Mayor, said it was the desire of the
citizens' committee to have a purely mili
tary parade In the day time and at night
a joint parade of the military and civic
societies, but if that did not meet the
views of the Native Sons and Daughters,
who had taken the initiative in the re
ception, the citizens' committee would do
all that could be done to work in har
mony with them.
-Mr. Martin said the citizens' committee
has in contemplation a banquet to the
soldiers, but that no arrangements would
I-.- made that would in any way Interfere
with the banquet the Native Sons and
Daughters have arranged.

Citizens' Executive Committee

_____ SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 8. 1899.
BOAT OWNERS WILL
SWELL THE FUND
THE executive council of the finance committee raising funds for the recep
tion of the returning volunteers was greatly encouraged in its work yes
terday by a statement made by Harbor Commissioner Kilburn. Several
days ago the Harbor Commission*; rs were appointed a committee to canvass
the owners of steamers, tugs, launches and Other small craft for the
purpose of ascertaining how many of them were willing to run excursions
on Reception day, turning over the proceeds to the reception committee.
Commmissioners Kilburn and Harney put in yesterday forenoon on the er
rand. The result is best told in Commissioner Kilburn's own words:
"We saw the owners of a great many small craft," he said, and not one
of them but promised to do as we asked. It all remains with the people to
swell the fund. Major Harney's Idea and mine is that excursionists be
charged 50 cents a head to go out on the bay to meet the transports. We
are satisfied that there will be at least 20,000 people willing to avail them
selves of the opportunity at that price. If that is the case it is only a matter
of carrying capacity for us to add $10,000 to the reception fund.
The entire matter will be settled to-morrow afternoon at 3 o'clock at a
meeting of shipowners in the Harbor Commissioners' office.
XV. J. Martin, chairman of the decoration
committee. He stated that his committee
was anxious, in order to proceed intel
ligently, to know how much money would
in- appropriated to it. On being asked
how much money would be required for
the night display. Mr. Martin replied at
least $6000. On motion of Mr. de Voting
the decoration committee, was authorized
to spend that amount, with the under
standing that if it is found possible later
to Increase the amount it will he done.
It was the general opinion that the
decollation question was the most im
portant one of all, and the committee was
Inclined to be generous on that point.
The $6000 will not Include the fireworks
display. In this connection the Chinese
Arm of "Wing Fat & Co. .set a good ex
ample by offering to donate Its surplus
stock of fireworks left over from Fourth
of July.
Mr. de Young presented the following
resolutions providing for a proper organ
ization of the executive committee:
Resolvedâ That no liabilities be in- i
curred by any committee or member without
th. proper authority from the executive com- i
mittee.
â¢ Secondâ That the secretary shall keep a rec- .
ord of all contracts entered into and all lia- ;
bilities incurred.
Thirdâ The sub-committees are only author
ized to spend moneys within the limitations
set by the executive committee and the
amounts appropriated for the purposes of said j
committee. -.*'.â¢â¢"
Fourthâ All bills shall lie presented to the j
executive committee to be audited and certi- i
fied by the chairman and secretary to the
treasurer, who alone will have power to pay i
them.
Fifth â Payments will be made by check in
every Instance except where the amounts are i
under $10.
NAMING THE- MEN TO
COLLECT THE MONEY
Executive Council o! the Finance
Committee Completes the
Working List.
At yesterday's meeting of the executive
council of the finance committee, to which
has been Intrusted the work of collecting
the funds for the reception of the home
coming volunteers, good progress was
made toward putting solicitors in the
field. The list of citizen committeemen
was completed and steps were taken to
get them into action as quickly as pos
sible. The following members of the com
mittee were in attendance: M. H. de
Young (chairman), Henry Sonntag, Paris
Kilburn, Julius Kahn, A. ,J. Raisch, Grove
Ayres, J. P. Jackson, A. H. Vail, George
H. Pippy (secretary).

Le Vinson, $50; Samuel Rosenheim, $20: Cal
; ifornia Saw Works, $25: I. K. D. Bird, $10.
Robert L. Park, past president; Poon
Gay, secretary-, and See Tv Gee, assistant
secretary, of the Chinese Native Sons,
were present to ask the committee to ap
point some night during the week when
it would be possible to send a couple of
its representatives to lay before the Chi
nese residents of the city a plan for the

SULTANS. SULU fIND UTfIH-
THE Mahometan Sultan of Sulu is rattling in the
pocket of his broad-hulled trousers $10,000 in
American gold and has on file a contract, made
in the name of the American people, for the payment
to him of an annual subsidy of $20,000 in the same
coin, as his reward for being a Yankee and acknowl
edging our jurisdiction. The taxpayers who furnish
the money may wonder why we paid Spain twenty
millions for the sovereignty of all those islands and
then pay the Sultan a salary to admit our title. The
presumption is that in default of the salary he would
have disputed our authority, and there is room for
still more speculation why we salary him and shoot
the Christian Filipinos.
Our agreement with him stipulates mat we musi
leave him undisturbed in the religion and institutions
of his people.; That religion is Mohametan, and the
institutions are polygamy and chattel slavery. While
we extend our jurisdiction over the Koran
and polygamy and salary the Sultan who be
lieves in the one and practices the other the
Stars and Stripes, protecting the harems of
Sulu, waves as a banner of virtuous vengeance to
strike terror into the harems of Utah. The Book of
Mormon and the continuing revelations that pro
ject it, like the Koran, teach polygamy as a religious
duty, not assumed exactly as a burden but rather as a
means of offsetting certain austerities in the obser
vance of creed and ritual. Pious as Mormondom is,
the Utah basin would smile if a brother should lead
his plural mates to the altar protesting against the
privilege as a burden and a frowning providence not
lined by a dollar's worth of silver.
The Book of Mormon is built on the Koran and
the Code Napoleon. It establishes in Utah one of
the institutions which we recognize and protect in the
Sulus. It is on the principle that surfeit of a good
thing is impossible, and throughout it converts to
highly spiritual purposes that cheerful song â
"When you get a good thing
Keep it, keep it."
Whereas the Koran stops at four wives per husband,
the Mormon establishment puts no limit on the
game. But, if it is a praiseworthy institution, de
serving a salary and distinguished consideration in
Sulu, what's a limit among friends? But the United
States, which looks upon plurality of wives in the
Sulus and calls it good, looks upon the same in Utah
-md calls it an abomination before the Lord! â¢
When Utah was admitted as a State the Unites
States made a contract with her which bears no. re
semblance to the one made with the Sulu Sultan
Congress bound Utah to observe the principles of the
Declaration of Independence and to forever abjure
polygamy or, plural marriage. Congress forced the
incorporation of the Tucker-Edmunds law into the
first statutes of the new State, fixing fine and im
prisonment as the penalty of keeping a harem. In
Utah there are twelve Sultans. They are called the
First Presidency of the Church, and are sometimes
known as the Twelve Apostles. One of them, the
Sultan Angus Cannon, determined to live up to hi
religion and obey the spirit rather than the statutes,
has been getting married at every drop of the hat for
the last year or two, until his harem numbers six
ladies. But his further career as a family man has
been cut off. The law has its grip on him, and he ha
pleaded guilty to an act which is repaid with a fine an;
jail in Utah and with an annual salary and protection
in the Sulus all under the flag and jurisdiction of the
United States!
The Utah Battery has just returned from the Phil
ippines. In it are many Mormons and a grandson of
Brigham Young. , They left the Sultan of Sulu in
good health, well and abundantly married and paid
a salary. They find one of the Sultans of Utah, also
well and abundantly married, pining in a dungeon
therefor. They also find the same flag over the Utah
jail that they have been engaged in nailing to the
Philippines. May we not expect a great emigration
movement from Salt Lake to Sulu?
The flattery of England has accomplished in the |
Transvaal what the force of arms could not do. Oom
Paul has agreed to settle his troubles with the aid of ;
diplomacy.
