HAWAIIAN LABOR.
1- J
IN an interview on the "wide open coolie and con
tract labor policy in Hawaii Senator Perkins de
clares that his act to prevent the importation of
coolies would have passed the Senate except for an
objection interposed by Senator Morgan of Alabama.
Senator Perkins, therefore, charges Morgan with the
responsibility of permitting the violation of every
promise that was made the American people by the
annexation crowd. It will be remembered that every
organ of annexation insisted that under the joint reso
lution of Congress, which effected the union of
Hawaii to this country, contract labor would be im
possible and coolies could not enter the islands. The
Call at that time pointed out the fallacy of this state
ment and predicted just what has happened. Nearly
30,000 coolies have been imported under penal con
tracts, and an energetic effort has been made to get
Italians and Portuguese under the same form of con
tract. Between coolie contract labor in Hawaii, as
now existing under c*ir flag and jurisdiction, and
slave labor in the South before the Civil War, if
there be any difference, it is in favor of the slave la
bor as far as the condition of the individual is con
cerned.
There has been talk in Honolulu of convening the
Island Legislature to repeal the penal clause in the
labor contract laws. It will be remembered that while
annexation was before Congress a bill to effect this
repeal was introduced in the Hawaiian Legislature,
and the annexation agents from the islands and the
American newspapers they had bribed gave the most
solemn assurances that the repeal should be passed.
Millions of Americans read this promise, and believe
even now that it was carried out, but it was not, nor
will it be until the people of this country force these
manipulators to keep faith.
The statement by Senator Perkins is of first impor
tance because it fixes responsibility for this condition
of things. Senator Morgan tried in vain to dictate a
foreign policy to Mr. Cleveland, and, failing, turned
and fought the administration of his own party with
a reckless disregard for truth and for the decencies of
official life that tarnished throughout the world the
reputation of public life in this republic. He has
wrought ceaselessly to impose his fantastic ideas of
foreign policy upon President McKinley's adminis
tration, and good Republicans have omitted any ex
pression of pleasure at seeing the counsel of men like
Senator Hoar and ex-Senator Edmunds rejected
while Morgan made himself a mouthpiece of an ad
ministration of which he is the natural political
enemy.
Morgan's party in the campaign next year will at- :
tack the Republicans upon the very policy which he
tried to force upon Cleveland, and it is well to bear
in mind the origin of that policy.
President McKinley sent Morgan as a Commis
sioner to Honolulu to make an examination of the
status in the islands and report a code to Congress
for the government of that country. This work was
supposed to be done, and at any rate was paid for by
the American taxpayers. It was expected by Ameri
can employers that the employers of labor in the
islands would be at once forced to assume legal
equality with them. It was also expected by Ameri
can laborers that their wages and their hours of labor
would not be imperiled by coolie contract competition
in Hawaii. And by the whole people it was expected
that the solemn promises made by the annexationists
would be kept. But every one of these expectations
has been disappointed. Every promise has been bra
zenly violated, and Senator Perkins states publicly
that Senator Morgan is responsible, and the people
believe him. The Democracy of California made a
ribboned ox out of Morgan when he was here. Their j
official and personal attentions to him were of the
highest order. His leadership was hailed in voices j
scented by every variety of spirits used to keep up j
party courage, from steam beer to Mumra. He es- !
pecially delighted them by coarse and ribald abuse of
Cleveland. Now it appears that instead of going to j
Hawaii to do his duty to the Republican administra- j
tion which honored him, and to the people who paid j
him, he went to consult the island tricksters and to I
concert ways and means for maintaining servile labor j
there, to the damage of American workmen, and re
turned to deceive the President who sent him by im
peding the performance of promises for which the
administration was responsible. The Republican
party, remembering the words of Lincoln, insists
upon equality before the law for everybody within the
jurisdiction of the United States.
When Great Britain abolished slavery Freedom
touched with her emancipating chrism every foot of
British soil. It became and has remained the boast
of British statesmen that the genius of universal
emancipation spread her wings over the empire, and
when the foot of a slave touched that soil his chains !
fell off and his bonds were broken.
Among the other national shames that are upon us
shall we have to blush for the betrayal of American
labor and denial to it of universal equality before the
law wherever the flag floats? That is the position in i
which the Morgan policy has put us. Will not the
leaders of the Republican party extricate us from the
control of this Alabaman Mokanna?
Deny it as he may, Emperor William can no longer
disguise his hostility to America and Americans. He
threw off the mask the other day when he proffered
the services of the skipper of the Meteor to sail the
Shamrock against the Columbia.
It will be difficult for headline writers on the great
dailies to refrain from referring to the walkout of New
York compositors as "Another Case of Sunstroke."
THE DUTY OF THE DAY.
ATTEND the primaries. Go to the polling
booths and vote for the candidates who repre
sent the better elements of citizenship. That
is the duty of every citizen; it is in a special sense
the duty of taxpayers, of property-owners, of indus
trious workingmen, of all who constitute the intelli
gence, honesty and the progressiveness of the com
munity.
Fair-minded Democrats can see no prospect of
good government in their party, dominated as it must
be either by the faction of Buckley or by that of
Rainey and the Examiner. Within the Republican !
party, however, there is a bright prospect of victory
for intelligence and honesty. If the citizens who have
a true regard for public welfare will act in accordance I
with that regard, all will be well. Good men will con
trol the Republican convention, and thus there will
be assured the nomination of a ticket which good men
of all parties can support against any ticket that may
be named by either the Buckleyites or the Rainey
iles of the Democratic camp.
The Call has no special set of men to champion.
Let each Republican ; go over the list of candidates
from his district and vote for those whom he knows
to be gcod and true. No man known as a tool of the
bosses or of the railroad should receive the support of
an intelligent voter. Such men cannot have the wel
fare of the community at heart, nor can they, be
counted on to vote in the convention for the nomina
THE SAN FSAiNCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 8, 1899.
tion of able, public-spirited men. It is, in fact, to de
feat just such candidates that good citizens should at
tend to their political duties.
Every taxpayer, every business man, every indus
trious workingman, has a personal concern in the
struggle of the day. An honest, progressive,: busi
ness-like administration of municipal affairs will be of
personal benefit to every good citizen. Bad govern
ment will entail deficient public services and heavy
burdens of taxation. For their own interests, there
fore, as well as from a sense of civic patriotism, all
should make it a point to take part in the primaries.
By the votes cast to-day it will be virtually deter
mined whether San Francisco is to have a good gov
ernment under the new charter or not. It is nou#
possible to elect good men to the Republican conven
tion and so assure a strong and reliable ticket. He
who is willing to act for the city as well as to talk
must act to-day, must attend the primaries to-day,
must vote for good men to-day. To-morrow it will be
too late.
The Pullman twins were advised the other day by
their mother that they would have to quit trying to
preserve themselves in alcohol or prepare to eke out
a rum-sodden existence in poverty. They started at
once to taper off. At last accounts George had suc
ceeded so well that he was rapidly becoming purple
in the face.
PLEDGES FAITHFULLY KEPT.
WITH the adoption of a tax levy within the
dollar limit the Republican majority of the
Board of Supervisors have kept faith with the
public on that issue, and the party can now point to
a record of official service remarkable for its fidelity
to campaign promises.
The subject merits the attentive consideration of
voters. As it is the rule to denounce an official who
proves false, so it should be the rule to give honor
to those who are faithful, and under that rule the
Republican Supervisors deserve and should receive
the commendations of their fellow citizens.
The Republican platform, after declaring in favor
of municipal ownership of public utilities, went on. to
.say: "Until that result is accomplished we pledge
our nominees for Supervisor* to fix a water rate which
will net the Spring Valley Water Company not more
than 5 per cent on its capital stock, providing that the
operating and constructing expenses of the corpora
tion be reduced to an amount that is fair and equal.
To that end we pledge our said nominees to make .1
careful and methodical investigation of the books of
the company.''
That pledge has been kept, and water rates both to
the municipality and to private consumers have been
reduced in accordance with it.
On the subject of the lighting supply of the city
the platform said: "The charge for gas and electric
light should be reduced and the quality improved,
and the city should have its lamps lighted every night
in the year. The lighting of the city constitutes a
large and necessary item of public expense. We fa
vor legislation which will encourage competition for
this contract and secure to the public the maximum
of efficient service at the minimum of expense. We
are in favor of placing underground all electric wires,
and we pledge our nominees to secure these results." <
The three demands set forth in that plank have been
complied with. The cost of lighting has been re
duced to the municipality and to private parties, the
Supervisors have provided for competition by grant
ing franchises to competing companies, and electric
wires are required to be put underground.
The platform asserted: "The affairs of the munici
pality should be conducted upon principles of hon
esty and economy that prevail, in private business.
Conducted on these principles a tax of one dollar per
hundred dollars of an assessed valuation of $350,000.
--000 will be amply sufficient properly to operate the
municipal government. We therefore pledge our can
didates for the office of Supervisor to limit the annual
tax levy to an amount not exceeding that which will
be produced by a tax of one dollar for every hundred
dollars of assessed valuation not exceeding
$350,000,000."
By the action taken yesterday that pledge is ful
filled, and after years of struggle the people will at
last, thanks to the Republican majority of the board,
have a fair test made of the dollar limit tax.
On the subject of assessments the platform de
clared: "We pledge our nominees for the Board of
Supervisors, sitting as a Board of Equalization, and
our nominee for Assessor, to the levying of all assess
ments upon an absolutely fair and equal basis. We
insist that as between rich and poor, corporation and
private citizen, there shall be no discrimination, that
the burdens of government shall be borne in equal
proportion by all classes, and that in the assessment
of corporate franchises care shall be taken to de
termine the reasonable value of such franchises in their
relation to more tangible property vested in the cor
porations -assessed by deducting the value of the en
tire tangible property of such corporations from the
market value of their stocks and bonds."
The Republican party did not elect the Assessor,
but the Republican majority of the Board of Equali
zation set itself earnestly to the task of revising the
assessments made by the Democratic incumbent of
that office. They increased the assessment of the
Market Street Railway Company, and would have
increased that of the Sutter street line and those of
other corporations had not the Democratic minority
by dilatory tactics prevented. Even with such ob
stacles to contend with the Republican Supervisors
acting as Equalizers have fulfilled that plank of the
platform to the extent at least of giving the city the
fairest assessment of property made within the life
of this generation.
, Such is the record of pledges fulfilled by the Repub
lican Supervisors. The six great issues which made
up the points of popular interest in the campaign last
year have been met in accord with the demands of the
people and the promises of the party. First, water
rates have been reduced. Second, gas and electric
rates have been reduced. Third, competition in gas
and ele-tric lighting has been encouraged and pro
vided for. Fourth, electric wires have been required
to be laid underground. Fifth, the dollar limit of
taxation has been adopted. â  Sixth, assessments have
been equalized better than ever before between the
private citizen and corporations, between the rich and
the poor.
On that showing of fidelity and honesty the Re
publican party can justly ask the continued support
of the voters in the municipal campaign.

NEW YORK HEIRESS
MADE- SCHOOL INSPECTOR
THE appointment of Miss Elsie Clews as school inspector for the Four
teenth District, New York, which has Just been made public, is a recogni
tion of the educational philanthropy the young heiress has practiced since
her graduation. On emerging from Barnard ln 1896 Miss Clews' first public
work was done ln the interests of the Citizens' Union and Seth Low's cam
paign for the Mayoralty. The Woman's Auxiliary of the union was* recruited
from New York's wealthy and exclusive classâ women who had dabbled in poli
tics only in the abstract, as a study of the aspects of the municipality.
Miss Clews' was one of the earliest signatures In the list of those publicly
pledged to support the union. She was one of the busiest workers ln the organiz
ing of meetings in all parts of the city, and was one of those on the platform at
the big mass-meeting in Cooper Union when ex-Mayor Strong was chairman
and Low, Choate, Waring and Carter among the speakers.
Miss Clews is a member of the League for Political Education, which contrib
uted so large a quota to the Union Auxiliary. The league has achieved Its political
ends, which are purely educational, largely through sociological means. The As
sembly district work, one of its specialties, brought its members into direct con
tact with the lives of the families of voters ln each district, with a view of improv
ing existing conditions and so helping to an Intelligent comprehension of muni
cipal needs.
Miss Clews' inspectorship will naturally prove of value in smoothing out school
difficulties. She is the only daughter of the banker, Henry Clews. Her mother has
been famous not alone as a belle but as one of the best-educated women of the
Four Hundred. ''. tftftf -'â¢'_;>"
Miss Clews herself holds the degree of doctor of philosophy, conferred by Co
lumbia. She is with her family at Newport.

Apache Volunteers A Lapse of
Memory.
Editor of The Call: Your paper this
morning announces a scheme on foot to
secure 10,000 Apache braves for use against
the Filipinos, for whose sole offense of
loving their country and hating, subjec
tion we are now at war with them.
In this It would appear that the Jingo
advocates of this benevolent scheme have
suffered another lapse of memory as to
the text of the supposed to be immortal
Declaration of Independence. Not that
their past record renders this further evi
dence at all surprising, but it furnishes
one more among an eloquent aggregation
of facts, showing >a mighty cleavage oil
their part, away from the principles of
just government. They either forget, or
ignore those passages of Jefferson s
matchless indictment against King
George, wherein it is charged:
"He has plundered our seas,
ravaged our coasts, burned our
towns and destroyed the lives of
our people.
"He has excited domestic in
surrection among us, and has en
deavored to bring on the inhabi
tants of our frontiers the merci
less Indian savages, whose known
rule of warfare is an undistin
guished destruction of all ages,
sexes and conditions."
Without claiming for the proposed dis
patch of savages to prey upon the Fili
pino patriots any greater degree of incon
sistency with American doctrine than has
marked the course of the Jingoes to date,
I would gladly learn by what alchemic
process an act becomes right when prac
ticed by McKinley upon the Filipinos
which was atrocious when practiced by
King George upon the Americans?
CLARENCE XV. ASIIFORD.
San Francisco. Aug. 7. 1899.
Give the Naval Reserve a Chance.
Editor The Call: California furnished
sixty-five men and ten officers from the
Naval Militia at San Francisco, who gave
up their homes and positions to serve for
Old Glory. They enlisted in a body at the
breaking out of the war and served
throughout on board the United States
steamers Iroquois and Vigilant. Although
these men did not see active service, they
were ready and went where the Secretary
of the Navy sent them. Ask Captain L.
H. Turner, who commanded the Iroquois
(formerly the tug Fearless), also Captain
Gunn of the Vigilant, and I am sure they
will have nothing but praise for their
men. Although they are the only volun
teers San Francisco* furnished from the
State militia besides our homeward
bound heroes, they are not Included in
any of the<festlvities mapped out. There
are seventy-five of them, nearly all of
whom since their discharge serving the
State on board the Marion.
If nothing more, they are at least en
titled to be included in the honorary es
cort of our home-coming heroes. Hoping
The Call will see justice done to her San
Francisco volunteer sailors, I am. very
respectfully, JOHN E. BROWN,
San Francisco, Aug. 6, 1899.

PRESS COMMENTS
What the Oregonians Say.
San Francisco Star.
While the Examiner confined Its Inter
views w"ih the returned Oregon volun
teers wholly to their opinions of the con
duct of the war, The Call has broadened
its interviews to the availability of the
islands for white labor and the character
of the people. Their views as to the en
tire unfitness of the climate for whites
are unanimous as to every one of the j
thirty-four men interviewed. .]
They differ as to the availability of the
Filipinos for citizenship, some holding
they would make excellent citizens and
others quite the contrary- Let them be
citizens of their own country and not of
ours is the consensus of opinion. In other
words, leave them alone, and they will
work out their own salvation.
In another column will be found ex
tracts from a letter to The Star from Mr. ;
Howard XV. Bray, exactly concurring
with these men as to the unfitness of the >
climate for white men.

Why the Examiner Quit.
Alameda Argus.
The Examiner started in to lay Chris
topher Buckley out, but when it received
a hunch to the effect that the blind boss
had possession of the political archives
of the past that paper knocked off and
turned its attention to borrowing over
coats for the Oregon boys.
Automobiles in New York
Solano Republican.
Speaking of the automobile, it is said
that there are 1500 of these "horseless car
riages" in operation in New York City.
From this it is intimated that the horse
will soon become one of the "things that
were."

THE FERRY' CLOCKâ J. C. F., Vallejo,
Cal. The minute hand on the ferry
clock, San Francisco, is twelve- feet long
: and the hour hand nine feet long. From
1 center to tip the minute hand is ten feet.
, BOOK MAKERâ O.'.S., City.' If a book
maker on a race track owns three or four
books, runs but one himself and has other
men to run the others, he owns all, but
controls only the one that he individually
runs.

A DEFACED BALLOT â Subscriber,
City. The instructions to voters are that
they must paste a slip under the party
head they wish to vote at the election to
day, and that if there is a name erased
on that slip and another substituted it
invalidates the ballot, and it will on the
count be thrown out.
THE WINNERâ M. W.. Oakland, Oal.
This correspondent asks a question that
has been answered a number of times in
this department, but like questions about
when the new century will commence
they never stop. The question is: "If in
a game of casino A wants eight to go and
B wants three and on the deal played A
gets cards, spades, little casino and Shree
aces and B gets big casino and one ace,
which wins?" In the absence of an agree
ment that points are to be counted as
made the one holding cards counts first,
and in this case A, who had cards, hav
ing made enough points to run out, wins
the game.
DISHONORABLY DISCHARGE
'Reader.-City. The law of California says:
Every member of the National Guard dis
honorably discharged from the military service
of the State of California shall be disfranchised
for the period of one year next ensuing such
discharge.
No dishonorably discharged non-commis
sioned officer, artificer, musician or private or
member of a company dishonorably discharged
from the National Guard shall be permitted
to again enter any company of , the National
Guard, except the offense is pardoned by the
commander In chief.
No dishonorably discharged officer of the
National Guard of California shall be permitted
to hold any office of trust or emolument, nor
be permitted to again enter any company of
the National Guard except the offense be par
doned by the commander in chief.
MARRIAGES AT SEAâ Reader, City. A
marriage on the high sea was, prior to the
act in this State prohibiting what was pre
viously to that time known as a contract
marriage, was such a marriage, or rather
was so recognized, notwithstanding that
a minister or Justice of the Peace united
the couple. It is claimed that outside of
territorial limits neither has any authority
to perform any official act. If â  such
"marriage" took place after the prohibi
tory act went into effect, then it is no
marriage at all and there would not be
any occasion for a legal divorce; the
parties would simply have to quit one
another. If before that "act. the party
wishing to separate would have to estab
lish proof of a contract marriage, and
there would have to be an action for a
divorce.

In offering the report of the r-inanpe

The report of the Finance Committee
did not deal lightly nor delicately with '

since the tax levy was first discussed. His
original message on that question pi< dgod
himself to the dollar limit, and he _Â»:bse

of the report, which recited his various
suggestions, he frantically attempted to
dodge all responsibility for the success or
non-success of the instrument, th text
of which he had practically framed.
The efforts of the Mayor to g'.-t from
under should his political dollar limit
schemes go wrong have been so pro
nounced the past fortnight that the com
mittee was constrained to state its posi
tion in its report. Upon that point it said:
His Honor the Mayor's opinions, he
being the exponent of the require
ments of the government under the
consolidation act and under the new
charter, have been given much
weight and his suggestions in the
main have been adopted in the con
sideration of retrenchments to be ef
fected, without reference to any other
consideration, political or otherwise.
As the responsibility for the main
tenance and operation of the govern
ment to be instituted next January
is freely assumed by his Honor the
Mayor, your committee certainly can
have no motive other than to second
his efforts to secure either under the
present or the new regime an eco
nomical and successful administra
tion of the public business.
"I object to that passage," shouted Mr.
Phelnn. "I have never assumed respon- I
sibility for the tax levy." j
"1 apologize to your Honor," retorted
Supervisor Perrault. "We have been led j
to believe from reading the Examiner ,
that you had pre-empted the Mayor's,
chair for the next term of offlce."
Upon Mr. Phelan's suggestion to reduce
the force and wages of City Hall janitors
and employes, the report of the commit
tee was particularly severe, so severe,
in fact, that the Mayor again rose in his
wrath to deny that he had ever said any
such thing. Dr. Perrault simply called
on the transcript of the shorthand re
porter to back him up. and that not be
ing forthcoming a decision went over
until the next meeting. In every case
where his name was mentioned or his
connection with the tax levy was hinted
at Mr. Phelan was' ready with a dis
claimer. He had submitted a levy and
the board had followed its suggestions so
far as It was practicable, but ne wanted
It understood that he was not responsible
fur what he had done, and said so in the
sharpest language.
The principal changes made by the com
mittee in its original schedule were in
the cases of the Health Department, the
Fire. Department and the City and County
Hospital. It increased the Health De
partment's fund $15.9'"0, or enough to pro
vide for the Ferry Hospital and the Food
Inspection Department. The City and
County Hospital fund was Increased from
$80,000 to $111,906 and $1305 was added for
repairs. The Almshouse fund was raised
from $75,000 to $90,000 and a high tribute
paid to Captain Beddy for his adminis
tration of the institution. The reduced
figures for the Tax Collector submitted
by the Mayor were agreed to, but the
water fund* was Increased from $250,000
to $259,000 and the Street Department was
raised from $353,000 to $402,204.
When the report came up for adoption
the Mayor demanded that the passages
he had objected to be stricken out. He
said no gentleman would make such
things a part of any report he might have
occasion to write. Supervisor Byington
thought so, too, and so did Mr. Deasy.

withdraw all personal allusion until such

BISHOP GAINES
TO PRESIDE IT
THE CONFERENCE
Annual Session of the
A.-M. E. Clergy.
WILL CONVENE TO-MORROW
Reception To-Night in Bethel M. E.
Church to the Visiting Colored
Ministers List of Stand
ing Committees.
The thirty-second session of the Cali
fornia African Methodist Episcopal An
nual Conference will convene to-morrow
morning in Bethel A. M. E. Church at
9:30 o'clock. Bishop XV. J. Games, D.D..
will preside during the deliberations of
the important body. The opening of the
conference will be an interesting feature.
Mayor Phelan will be present and will
deliver the welcome address on behalf of
the city.
Bishop Games, who will preside, is well
known throughout the country ,aB the
great leader of his race. He enjoys the
highest esteem of his brethren who will
gather In this city from al! over the State
to it-nder an account of t!;_r stewardship
for the last twelve months.
! A reception will be tendered to-night to
j the visitors. An excellent programme has
J been ai ranged for the occasion* and it Is
, thought the church will be crowded. The
I daily sessions of the conference will be
j from 9:30 a. m. to 1 l). in. Various com
mitter will hold their meetings in the
afternoon. Rev. J. C. Edwards of Los
i Angeles will deliver the annual sermon
lon Wednesday evening at 8 o'clock. The
! missionary sermon on Thursday evening
; will be delivered by Rev. W. W. Grimes
lof Sacramento. There will be a big edu

FAREWELL TO
SERVICE, SAY
OREGON MEN
The Regiment Mus
tered Out.
MEN AND OFFICERS PAID OFF
Lively Scenes Out at the Presidio
During the Breaking of the
Northern Boys'
Camp.
The closing scenes of the military life j
i of the Second Oregon Volunteers were en- j
i acted yesterday. The men were given j
! their discharges, they were all paid off ,
I and by nightfall the camp that has. been
'so busy was almost deserted. Most of the
! men spent the night in town. A few came
I back to the camp and spent the night
. there after passing the first hours of free
dom in the city, but out of the whole ra- ,
iment there were not more than one hun
dred to be found in the tents two hours
after the last company had been mustered
out.
Before the actual mustering out of the
men began General Summers lined up his
regiment and addressed his soldiers. He
spoke of the cordial relations which hud |
existed between the men and their oitt- |
cers, the willingness of the regiment to
obey orders ana perform any duty and j
of the record it had made. He then ap- j
pealed to the men to remain together until j
they should reach home. He wished to
take the regiment back to Its own State
i just as it had left a year ago, and he
j asked the men to stay together and not
! to separate at the last moment.
' Then the last piece of work was taken
up. Each company was formed in line
and the mustering officer called the roll ,
| and verified the papers of every man as ;
â¢ be answered to his name. Each man was
j handed his discharge, and then th^ com- j
j pany was marched before the paymaster, j
who closed each man's account and paid ,
him the balance due him. General Sum- .
! mers was the last man in the regiment to |
! receive his pay and his discharge.
There were over 1100 men mustered out,
and they were paid altogether over -i'".-i
' 000, or an average of about $127. Th. men .
! were paid for one month and seven days'
I service since last payday, two months' i
I extra allowed by Congress to every man
i serving outside the country during the
war, a balance due on clothing allowance ;
and travel pay home.
The men are not satisfied with their dis
charges and there is a vigorous compiatnt
taking shape among the better element of
the regiment. Every discharge bears in
the blank for the performance of duty i
the words "honestly and faithfully." No i
distinction has been mad.- between the !
good and the bad. and there is no blank I
for conduct or character as Is In the dis
charge granted to men having the regu- I
lar service. The men of the Oregon regl-. I
ment say there has been nothing to show
who of the regiment have tried to keep
up the reputation of the organization and
have done their best to fit themselves for
their duties. No distinction has been made
between the proficient and the inefficient ]
j and the proficient believe they have the j
I right to have their efforts to make good \
! soldiers of themselves made of record In ;
i their discharges. A number have come <
I together and have drawn up a protest i
| against the single classification of the ;
I men, and this will be prerenied to Gen- j
! eral Summers It is probable that the
â¢ matter will be taken before the Governor
of Oregon and that he will do something
in the matter.
As soon as the first company had gone
through the mustering -"it mill signs of
i departure began to appear on every side. \
| In the vicinity of the post an astute busi- ,
I ness man had laid in a stock of trunks
and upon these the men made an assault. j
His stock was barely sufficient to stand
the sudden drain upon it and before long [
there were trunks all over the camp, al'
In different stages of packing. It was an
nounced to the men that trucks would be â¢
in readiness this morning to cart the bag
gage to the ferry, but the men would not ;
wait. They sent for wagons of their own
and all day loads of trunks and boxes
blocked the' streets leading from the Pre- {
sidio. Lieutenant Knapp, quartermaster
of the regiment, had ordered eight trucks
: to report at the camp this morning, hut
as he saw the amount of baggage grow
ing less and less be countermanded the i
order for eight and made it three. It Is I
hardly probable that even three will be
filled " by what is left. The tents are
empty, the property Is nearly all away,
: and 'he deserted company streets are be
ing patrolled by a guard from the Penn
sylvania regiment.
The men will report out at the Presidio |
1 this morning in time to form In line at 1
o'clock to march to the ferry. It Is op
tional with them, of course, but General
! Summers says he will have a full regi
i ment in line. They will be escorted by the
band of the Nebraska regiment and the
Utah artillerymen. They will have no
arms, but will carry their colors. The
march will be down the streets by which
they reached the Presidioâ down Lombard ;
street to Van Ness avenue, to Golden |
Gate avenue, to Market street and to the ;
ferry. The regiment will march on board I
the Oakland boat at once and on the |
mole it will take the special train wnich i
will be waiting there. Then with a whoop I
and a hurrah the boys will off for Ore
gon. * . ,_
General Summers expressed the pro
foundest gratitude and appreciation of the !
way in which he and his regiment have â 
been treated by the city of San Fran
cisco.' It will be one of the bright spots j
ln the history of the regiment, he said,
and he spoke for every one of his men .
when he said they would never forget it. !

THREE FEDERAL DECISIONS, j
Ninety-Day Clause in Accident In
surance Declared to Be Valid
and Binding.
United States Circuit Judge Morrow de
livered an opinion yesterday in favor of
the defendant in the case of Lucy Pryor
Brown against the United States Casualty
Company. The suit was brought by the
widow of Arthur Page Brown, architect,
to recover $10,000 on two policies of acci
dent insurance for $5000 each.
It was admitted by the defendant that
Brown dltd from the direct result of a
runaway accident on January 21, I_Â«0, 106
days after the accident. The company
paid the widow $"".7 14. being $50 weekly
indemnity from the time of the accident
to the time of Mr. Brown's death, but
refused to pay the death benefits, alleging
as a reason for such refusal the existence
in the policies of a clause providing that
1 death benefits shall be paid only in cases
I where the death occurs within ninety days
after the accident.
Judge Morrow held that the clause was
! valid and that the company was not i
! obliged to pay the death benefit.

MRS. ARATA DROWNED.
The Body of the Demented Woman
Found Floating in the Bay.
Mrs. Louisa Arata of 716 Montgomery
avenue, who left home on July 16 after
repeated threats of suicide, made her word
good, and her disfigured body was found
yesterday morning floating in the bay at
section 1 of the seawall.
Several months ago an Infant son of Mr.
and Mrs. Stephen Arata died of the croup.
The father at the time was In Italy, and
the mother became afflicted with the idea
that on his return to this city he would
blame her for the child's death. She
grieved so much over the matter that her
brain gave way. and at 11 o'clock oil the
night of July 16 she slipped out if the
house unknown to her husband and dis
appeared. The condition of the oody in
dicates that she drowned herself on the
night on which she left home. *_
The remains were taken to the Morgue
and an inquest was held yesterday morn
ing, the jury returning a verdict that Mrs.
Arata had committed suicide while tem
porarily insane.

COMMERCIAL AND FINANCIAL NEWS
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver and financial quotations unchanged.
Wheat and Barley inactive and rather weak.
Oats freely offered and easy. Com and Rye dull.
Hay continues to arrive liberally. Feedstuff's unchanged.
Beans quiet at unchanged prices. Seeds rule dull.
Potatoes and Onions steady at previous prices.
Vegetables in heavy supply and cheap.
Butter advanced again.
Cheese steady and Eggs easy and quiet.
Grapes and Melons in larger supply and weaker.
Peaches, Pears and Plums stand about the same.
Nothing new in dried fruits.
Provisions still in demand at firm prices.
Hogs easier but no lower. Other Meats unchanged.
Two cars of Eastern Poultry on the market.
New prices for Pacific Codfish.
Charters.
The Alhert and S. G. Wilder load merchandise
for Honolulu.
The Kilmory was chartered prior to arrival
for wheat from Portland to Europe, 37s 6d.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECASTS.
The pressure continues low over the greater
portion of the Pacific Slope. The usual Bum
mer low prevails over Arizona and a trough
extends northward through Nevada, Joining a
second low over Idaho.
The temperature has risen slightly over
Utah nnd Southern Nevada. It has fallen over
Oregon and Southern Washington. Through
out California the temperatures are from six
to eight degrees below the normal.
Showers are reported in Northern Nevada
and in Idaho. '
At El Paso the maximum wind velocity was
reported as 3s miles from the northwest.
Forecasts made at San Francis for 30 hours
ending midnight. August S. 1899:
Northern CaliforniaâCloudy Tuesday: warm
er in the Sacramento Valley: fresh northwest
erly winds.
Southern California-Fair Tuesday: fresh
westerly winds.
Nevadaâcloudy Tuesday, with showers early-
Tuesday morning.
UtahâCloudy Tuesday; probably light thun
der showers.
fArizonaâ Cloudy Tuesday.
San Francisco and vicinityâCloudy: warmer
Tuesday, with fog in the morning: fresh west
erly winds. ALEXANDER G. McADIE,
Forecast Official.

I EASTERN MARKETS.
Â«__ i
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK. Aug. 7.â To-day's stock market
was largely professional and seemed to move
in response to the closing out of speculative ac
counts rather from any new ventures. A group
of specialties which were lifted last week re
acted sharply on profit-taking, while those that
had been raided by the bears last week tended
toward very. The Tobacco stocks and
Tennessee Coal ,were most acute sufferers on
realizing. American Tobacco fell off 2*. points,
with only a fractional rally, and Tennessee
Coal dropped an extreme 2"4- The Continental
tobacco stocks and securities of companies di
rect I v or indirectly ln the steel trade were also
atfecled There was a continued upward move
ment in Kansas City, Pittsburg and Gulf, the
extreme advance reaching 4'Â«. This stuck be
came feverish in the late trading and i I Â«ed
with a net gain of I*4: The announcement of
the purchase of receivers* certificates by the re
organization committee may have to do with
the strength. Support was manifest in the
Wisconsin Central Issues, and th.- Pittsburg.
C. ,- and St. Louis stock 6 closed I@2 points
higher respectively on the da . There was a
Etrong upward movement in Northern Pacific
from the extreme rise, reaching I*4. This in
duced bear covering in Union Pacific, resulting
in an advance of a point in that stock. The
smelting stocks were very firm, and in the late
dealing's New York Central was lifted *4 on the
good showing of July earnings, while Penn
sylvania moved a point in the opposite direc
tion. Lackawanna and Paper dropped 2. each.
The attitude of speculators was for the most
-.art a waiting one. and there was no severe
pressure to sell, notwithstanding the low state
of reseives shown by Saturday's bank state
ment. Money continued in supply at 4 per cent.
The bank holiday in London left to means of
gauging the money market there, but the ad
\anre from 4*-_ to 5 per cent of German Im
perial banks rates of discount reflected the con
tinued process of drawing in of credits to the
London center. This did not prevent a fall of
a fraction of the rates for sterling exchange In
New York.
There was no sign of acute apprehension over
the money situation in the stock market, but
Interest is keenly centered on the money re
sources of outside domestic centers with a view
to estimating the demands which will be made
, -.. â¢<â -.: York for moving crops. Kansas City.
Pittsburg and Gulf firsts rose 4 per cent on
heavy purchases and reacted 2 per cent. Other
wise" the bond market was dull and heavy.
Total sales, $2,440,000. United States nev.- fours
advanced *_ in the hid price.

Chicago Grain Market.
<$â ________<â¢*>
CHICAGO, Aug. 7.â The most prominent fea
ture of wheat trading throughout the entire
session was its exceeding dullness. Interest In
trading, never very great during the hot sum- |
mer months, dwindled to insignificant propor- j
tions to-day, and the sum total of the ses
sion's transactions was hardly as goat as has
frequently been seen during a lively half hour.
Variations in the price were confined to a
range of %c in September and but a shade over
that In the more distant futures. September i
opened %c to %c lower at 69% c. Weather in
the spring wheat belt was favorable and this I
caused a little languid selling at first, enough I
to put the price at 69c. The favorable weather j
was In a measure offset by reports of disap- |
pointing yields from various points and by bad
crop reports from European countries. A mod- |
crate covering movement ensued, carrying Sep
tember to 69*-ic. Up to the noon hour the price
kept close to that figure, but after that time
the neglected market developed more weakness,
September getting back to 69c again. This was
"put" price, and several little rallies occurred
on buying against the privileges, but the sup
port was unsubstantial and the market invari
ably worked back to the even figure. The cash
demand was small and New fork reported only
ten boatloads taken for export. Primary re
ceipts continued large, the total being 756,000
bushels. September was bringing 69c at the
close.
Corn was Irregular, with some degree of j
activity.. The market opened weak on the ex- i
oellent" crop conditions and large crop esti
mates. The cash situation was strong, and
September showed a substantial advance at one
time. The new crop features were sold more
liberally, and later in the session September
also weakened, practically all the advanve be-
Ing lost. September closed a shade higher, and
December closed %c to Ur lower.
Oats were neglected. The market was In
fluenced almost entirely by corn. September
closed unchanged.
Provisions were dull, but rather steady. In
view of a break In hog prices, showing weak
ness early; recovered later and closed steady.
The demand for meats was good. Packers were
moderate sellers. At the close September pork
was 2%c lower, lard a shade lower and ribs a
shade higher.
The leading futures ranged as follows:

Wheat and Other Grains.
WHEATâ Chicago opened dull, with a down
-1 ward tendency in the absence of support. The
j sentiment was extremely bearish and the
j weather was favorable for the growing crops.
Small Russian shipments were the only encour
aging feature. Later on the market steadied
somewhat unaer Continental bids, as Spain and
Portugal were again buyers, but the rallying
tendency was checked by an increase of 910,000
bushels In the visible supply. Exporters, how
! ever, seemed to expect a good business before
I long. The weather was reported perfect for
Corn.
The local market was dull and featureless.

THE STOCK MARKET.
The week opened with an active business in
securities, but the only change on the morn
ing session was a slight advance in Hana
Plantation to $16 -â â â 
In the afternoon Giant Powder advanced to
$72 75. other changes being slight.
Mining stocks were firm and rather higher
than on Saturday. The telegram from the
pump said: "At 6:30 this morning the water
was 22 feet 4 inches below the lÂ»50-foot level
station, the water having fallen steadily dur
ing the last twenty-four hours. The elevator
has been working continuously."
The Justice and Ophlr assessments fail de
linquent In office to-day.
The Consolidated New York Mining Company
has re-elected the old directors, with Charles
Hlrschfeld as president, Thomas Cole vice pres
ident and Charles E. Elliott secretary.
The Paauhua Plantation Company has de
clared Its sixth consecutive monthly dividend
of 30 cents per share, payable on the :oth
The Mammoth mine of Utah paid a dividend
of 140,00 on the Ist. .

paid its first dividend of *_ per cent on tne let.
The amount was $1562.

idend of 50 cents per share on the loth.

cents, payable on the 15th.
In the Sierra -Nevada, mine, on the 1400
level the south drift started from the main
west' drift opposite the north drift was ad
vanced 28 feet; total length, 255 feet; face in
soft porphyry with seams and bunches of
Quarts. The quartz shows assays from $2 to
$8 per ton. East crosscut No. 3, from the
north drift from a point 60 feet south from
the east crosscut No. 2. has been advanced 14
feet; total length. 44 feet; face In porphyry
with clay slips. 900 levelâ The past week has
been devoted to timbering and repairing the
north drift. i'~"_-'-i, Â., . _ â 'â ''â¢'â : -
The official report of the Consolidated Cali
fornia and Virginia mine for the week end
ing August 5 Is as follows: 1400 levelâ The west
drift has been extended 25 feet, making 'Its
total length 770 feet. The formation passed
through is porphyry with occasional bunches
of quartz. 1650 levelâ No work has been done
on this level during the week. 1750 levelâ
The south drift on the sill floor was cleaned
out and retlmbered IS feet; total length from
starting point, 42 feet. Work in the face sus
pended for the present. At a point 10 feet
back from the face of this drift we have
started a west crosscut and advanced it eight
feet. When this crosscut is sufficiently ad
vanced we will upraise from It to connect with
the slope or raise cut into by the southwest
drift from the second floor of the stopes. par
ticulars of which were given in the weekly
report of July 22. The raise from the fourth
floor in- the stopes has been carried up two
sets and two sets wide. The top sets are now
west of the old stupes, and in quartz. Out
side of a cross streak four Inches wide, which
assays $23 per ton. the quartz assays from $1 50
to $5 per ton. ISOO levelâ south drift on
this level has been cleaned out and retlmbered
for a distance of 29 feet and stopped temporar
ily; total length, 175 feet; face is in material
assaying from $3 to $7 per ton. During the
week we saved from this drift 22Vs tons of
ore of an average assay value of $16 31 per ton.
From the south drift, at a point 80 feet south
of the wtnze station, a west crosscut has been
started and extended six feet: face In a mix
ture of quartz and porphyry. This crosscut
will pass under that section of ground where
lately ore has been extracted from on and
above the 1750 level. At a point 80 feet south
of the west crosscut an east crosscut has been
started and is out six feet: face in quartz,
clay and porphyry. This crosscut is intended
to connect with a winze that was sunk from
the 1750 to the 1950 level, and which is about
30 feet east of the south drift. 1950 stationâ
The pumpbob of the old Cornish pump was
taken out and two new station sets have been
put in, and the work of putting the station
ln good condition continues. The water to-day
(Saturday) in the shaft is 20 feet below this
station. Total ore extraction for the week,
22% tons, assaying $16 31 per ton, and 20 tons
assaying $6 per ton.

NOTE â the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are . additions to the sounuings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign ( â precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

WON THE PRIZE
For His Composition Upon the Father
of His Country.
Bobby has just reached English compo-
sition in his school, and his father is a
newspaper man, who prides himself on his
concise style. Bobby came home from
school the "other day in high glee because
his teacher had praised his composition)
on George Washington. He showed the
production to his father with pride, but
was somewhat crestfallen at his parent's
criticism: Â '..._-; Â«â  â¢
â¢â¢Too many words, my son. Too many
words altogether. Why can't your teach-
ers instruct you how to express your
ideas tersely? Now, just sit down at that
table, take this pencil, cut out every word
you can spare without leaving out a single
idea, and if your mother does not agree
with me that the result is better than this
composition which your teacher praises,
I'll give you half a dollar."
The lad took the pencil and fell to work
while his father read the paper. After a
lung time Bobby brought the heavily
scored manuscript to him, saying:
"It was hard work to keep In something
on every one of the things Washington is
famous for, dad. but I guess I did it.
This is how the result read:
"George Washington became the father
of his country because he had no little
boy of his own to whip for cutting down
cherry trees, .and he is remarkable among
American statesmen because he would
not tell a lie."
Mamma awarded the half-dollar to
Bobby.-New York Tribuna.ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.

TOOK TWO METHODS
OF ENDING HIS LIFE
OAKLAND, Aug. John Curley passed
his â¢her ln the back yard of their home
this morning without speaking, and went
into the house. A moment later a shot was
heard, and the father, on opening the
door, saw his son lying dead on a sofa.
John Curley was a teamster about 30
years â â¢Id living at Forty-sixth and Grove
streets. He did not return home last
night, but about noon to-day he appeared
at the house. He said nothing to any
body, but calmly walked through* the
yard into the house and committed sui
cide by firing a bullet Into his brain.
Short as was the time between leaving
his father and sister, who was also In
the house, and the firing of the shot.
Curley made two attempts to end his
life. As soon as he went Into the room
he made two severe cuts on his throat
â¢with a razor and a few seconds later used
his pistol. An nation of the body
showed that the gashes would have been
fatal, had they not received immediate
attention, but bleeding to death was evi
dently too tedious for Curley.
The deceased's father says that his son
has not seemed to be possessed of his
ordinary good sense for several weeks.
When he passed him In the yard his son
held up his coat with one hand as though
he were anxious not to pee his father.
Some years ago John Curley had trouble
with a man named Leggett, and shot the
latter in self-defense.
BLIND PEOPLE MARRY,
NOW APPEAL FOR AID
OAKLAND, Aug. 7.â Mrs. Mary J. Libb.
a blind young woman, appeared before
the Supervisors to-day as an indigent.
From her testimony it appeared that she
was formerly an Inmate of the Home for
the Adult Blind and that she had some
time ago married a man also blind. Nei
ther of them had been able to secure em
ployment, and to relieve them from a life
of squalor in a ramshackle hut at Thirty
sixth and Grove streets she pleaded for
aid. -'-"--
Among the other applicants for aid as
Indigents was R. F. Fallmer of '.*".*"' Union
street, Alameda. He Is the father of
Clara Fallmer, who shot and killed her
lover in Alameda xifcarly^wo years ago
and was afterward acquitted at great cost
to the county. Besides this daughter he
has five other children depending upon
him for support.
Both cases were referred to the Super
visors of the respective districts.
SHE BEGGED FOR NICKELS;
OWNED THREE HOUSES
Oakland office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Aug. 7.
Mrs. Margaret Olvis, the aged woman
who was arrested by Officer Woods last
Saturday night because she was found
begging on the streets, and on whom,
when searched, was found an old black
glove containing $14 55, pleaded guilty to
day in the Police art, and Judge Smith
sentenced her to three months' imprison
ment in the County Jail.
It developed in court when she was ar
raigned that she had insulted several
women on Washington street. Asked why
she had done this, Mrs. Olvis replied that
they "had refused to help her."
Mrs. Olvis is GO years of age. and has
been a familiar figure on tho streets. She
is a plump little woman and a perfect
actress when It comes to affecting a de
crepit condition. But when she is refused
alms she casts aside her disguise and
abuses in vile terms those who thus of
fend her. It is no secret that she pays
taxes on at least $300. worth of property,
being the owner of three cozy cottages on
Seventeenth street, from which she gains
a fair revenue; but all this she vigorously
denies, and says it is a shame teat the
widow of a Mexican war veteran should be
Incarcerated in a prison with disreputable
characters and criminals.
"Oh, I once had money," she frankly
confessed when questioned at the County
Jail this afternoon, "but it's all gone now.
aid the $14 they found on me was Just a
little thai was left over. I made my
money at cooking. .'';.:.
"No. 1 have no one to help me. I did
have a husband, Daniel Olvis. He was a
veteran of the Mexican war, but several
years ago. after 1 hadn't heard from him
for nearly a whole year, 1 received a tele
gram announcing that he had died at
X*kiah. I went there, but never found a
grave, and for all i know he has probably
married another woman."
MORE ARCHITECTURAL
PLANS HAVE ARRIVEI
BERKELEY, Aug. 7.â Late this after
noon the secretary of the Board of '*â¢
gents of the University of California re
ceived a consignment of drawings for the
Phebe Hearst international architectural
competition, which is to be decided in
Berkeley shortly. To-day's consignment
was the second of the ten which are
due to arrive here before the end of the
present month.
The plans were forwarded by the firm
of Whitney Warren and Charles D. Wet
more, architects of New York city. They
had a valuation of $1000. Judging from
the K lze of the packing case In which
they were shipped the drawing must be
of the most extensive and elaborate kind,
as the case completely filled the express
wagon which hauled it from the South
ern Pacific depot on ttuck avenue to
South Hall upon the university campus.
Acting Secretary James M wen took
charge of the Immense designs and had
them stored In the basement of South
Hall along with those already forwarded
by Philip Codman of Boston.
It is expected that the remaining eight
of the plans yet to arrive will be here
shortly, as the competition closes by the
end of August. As soon as possible after
that date the hoard of judges selected for
the important duty of choosing the
gigantic plans for the future University
0-~Californla will convene at Berkeley to
inspect the designs and make their de
cision.

DEATH RECALLS A
MAN'S PECULIARITIES
OAKLAND. Aug. 7. âA. H. White,
who was found dead In his bed at
Concord, . Contra Costa County, on
Saturday, was known in this city for
several peculiar reasons. Last April
he ordered a suit of clothes from a
local tailor, saying he Intended to get
married, but he never called for the
clothes nor paid for them. Some time ago
he made a sensational affidavit against
the Union Hotel saloon, in East Oakland,
before the City Council and then left the
city suddenly. He was a painter by oc
cupation, a widower, aged 45 years, and
leaves two children residing in the State
of Washington.
CAPTAIN GOODALL'S FUNERAL.
OAKLAND, Aug. The remains of the
late Captain Goodall, who died at the age
of 74 years in ids native town, Drycott,
county of Somersetshire, England, on
July 13, passed through this city this
morning en route to San Francisco. The
remains were accompanied by the widow.
The arrival was delayed two days on ac
count of several serious washouts along
the Denver and Rio Grande in Colorado
which tied up all the trains.
The funeral services have now been
definitely arranged to take place next
Wednesday afternoon at 1 o'clock from
the Simpson Memorial Church In San
Francisco. The interment, however, will
be in Mountain View Cemetery on this
side of the bay.

George Marston Accidentally Shot.
BERKELEY, Aug. George Marston.
a young man living at the corner of
Tenth street and Channlng way, West
Berkeley, accidentally shot himself with
a revolver this evening. Young Marston
was unloading the weapon when the piece
was discharged, sending a bullet through
his right hand and right knee. Dr. Glad
ding dressed the wounds, both of which
may prove quite serious.

They've Kissed and Made Up.
OAKLAND, Aug. 7.â The divorce suit of
Otille Walchow against Thomas Walchow,
wherein the plaintiff, among other things,
alleged that her cruel spouse on one oc
casion set her hair on fire, was dismissed

this, together with the accrued costs of
court, was promptly and cheerfully paid
by the husband.

THE ..SAN. ' FRAN CISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 8, 1893.
ELEVEN LAWYERS
AND AN EX-WIFE
CHASING HIM
Surprise in Freeman
Divorce Case.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, Aug. 7.
"I will never be able to pay any alimo
ny with eleven lawyers and this woman
behind me chasing me all. day long. I have
told Mrs. Freeman several times that
whenever she draws down the lawyers
| and lets me alone long enough to get a
start in business I will do what I can for
; her."
The speaker was Thomas Freeman, a
: carriage painter, in the employ of W. B.
Woodward, who had been -ited to appear
before Judge Ogden this afternoon to
show cause, if any he had, why he should
not be punished for contempt of court in
having failed and refused to pay his di
vorced wife, Sarah Freeman, $20 monthly
alimony until there was $320 due.
Mrs. Freeman was granted her divorce
by Judge Greene in January, 1*.95, on the
ground of infidelity and ever since then
Â«he hag had any amount of difficulty to
collect the alimony. On the witness stand
to-day the ex-wife testified that her for
mer husband hau been working every day
and that he surely must be able to con
tribute the small amount of $20 as de
creed for her support and maintenance.
Mrs. Freeman was formerly Mrs. Barnes
and she was also Freeman's second wife.
He has one son by his first wife and she
has two daughters by her first husband.
"Were It not for my two children," said
Mrs. Freeman to-day, "I would be com- :
pelled to go Into the streets for charity."
On cross-examination Attorney Frick
sprung a decided and rather sensational
surprise. He asked Mrs. Freeman to give
the date and place of her marriage to
Freeman and to this question her attor
ney J. E. McElroy, made serious objec
tions, 'but Attorney Prick explained to
the court that he intended bringing out
facts on which his client proposed to base
an action for the modification of the de
cree, intimating that the parties were
never legally married. Mrs. Freeman was
allowed to answer. She â¢J'ld that the
marriage was solemnized at San Diego In
September, 18S7.
The defendant was also called as a wit
ness to show that he had not earned
enough to meet the demand for alimony.
He paid he had earned but WOO the past
six months; that out of this he paid for
clothes, medicine, room rent, tools, etc..
about $-"--' When asked what had become
of the balance, $7*. he facetiously replied
that he "had eaten that." -ytf'"' _:'â¢.
Continuing, witness testified that at the
divorce trial he had shown that for
months he was compelled to go almost

On cross-examination Freeman admitted

would 'iron the divorce suit he would

This, however, she had refused, and the
offer was not repeated to-day.
Owing to the absence of an important
witness further hearing went over until
next Monday, by which time interesting
developments might be expected.
HOSPITAL OPENS TO-MORROW.
I The Board of Health announced yester
day that the Central Receiving Hospital
would be open again on Wednesday morn
i ing. The plumbing which was begun last
week will be completed to-night and the
building will be In sanitary condition.
'â  Only the repainting will remain to be fin
ished but as that is to be done ward by
ward there will be no inconvenience.
REMORSE CAUSED HIS ACT.
Richard Koch, one of the proprietors of |
the Sutter saloon at Market and Sutter
streets committed suicide by turning on
the gas in a room at 43 Hardy street yes
terday afternoon. The cause of his act
was trouble which he recently had with
his wife. â , _ _ tf.
Koch was about 30 years of age and had
been married five years. He frequently
abused bis wife and was arrested for
wife-beating about a month ago. After
his release domestic infelicity began anew
and three days ago Mrs. Koch left her
husband and home. Koch brooded some
what over the matter, and retired yester
day to his room, where he took his life.
The odor of gas was first detected by E.
T M. Lai?., who occupies the flat imme
diately above the one utilized by the Koch
family. McLain attempted to ascertain
whether there was any one in tip* house,
and receiving no response notified the
police. An officer smashed in the door and
repairing to a back room found Koch
stretched out on the bed, lifeless. The gas
had been escaping for some time.
Deputy Coroners La Combe and Flan
nigan were called and took the body to
the Morgue. The wife could not be found
last night. Koch was a prominent Ger
man and was associated with Theodore
Relnke in the saloon business.
REILLY HAD A FIT.
The Convicted Soldier Creates a Scene
in the City Prison.
John Reilly. a soldier who Is confined
in the City Prison, created considerable
consternation among his cellmates last
evening by screaming at the top of his
voice and otherwise acting like an insane
man. Sergeant Brown, thinking that he
had been suddenly bereft of his reason,
ordered him removed to the insane ward
of the Receiving Hospital. The superin
i tendent of the Institution refused to re
ceive him as he concluded that Reilly
! was shamming. Reilly was then returned
-, to the prison and locked up. Shortly
afterward he recovered his reason and
i explained that he had had a fit.
Illy was arrested Saturday night for
'â â  soliciting alms from pedestrians on Mar-
I ket street and was yesterday convicted
1 and sentenced to thirty days' Imprison
j ment in the County Jail by Judge Gra

MILITARY DRAMA
AND A PATHETIC
CENTRAL FIGURE
Brother Officers'" Is
a Success.
Trevor's London success, "Brother Of- I
ficers," was last night offered to a San j
Francisco audience by Henry Miller and !
his company at the Columbia and ac- j
cepted with applause and many curtain j
calls. To those who have eyes and will
see the pathetic heart story of "Brother j
Officers" is laid bare in the first act. \
Lieutenant John Hinds, a great-hearted,
strong-souled fellow, a diamond in the i
rough, sprung from the gutter, but with
"the feelins" of a gentleman, having
passed with distinction through the ranks
to a commission, Is suddenly set down in
the midst of swelldom in the persons of
the First Lancers and Its guests, whose :
ancestors, to a man, we are made to feel, I
bequeathed them the priceless legacy of j
an aristocratic name.
The feeling that he has, up to this, ;
missed the best companionship, is evoked
in sudden agony, as he touches, for the
first time, the hand of a beautiful, cul- â 
tured woman; Lady Roydon. He has i
been distinguished for great bravery in
the ranks, has, by daring courage, saved ;
the life of his "brother officer," Lieuten
ant Launcelot Pleydell; he has the soul
of a hero and the discipline of his com
panionship Is to make men of more than I
one of his comrades. Yet there he stands, .
a most pathetic figure, restless, 111 at
ease feeling his shortcomings, not know
ing 'his worth, telling Launcelot, when
they are alone, that he wants to be
taught manners In order-dressing, talk
ing eating, smoking and the rest. It s
a matter ot breed," he says, "we come
from different stables." And Launcelot
promises to lick him Into shape. In ,
obedience to the hitter's advice to be ;
genial and easy with the officers of the
mesa lords and all, he goes through his j
whole repertory of manners, and In try- ,
ing to do Justice to the occasion makes)
the awful mistake of asking "his dean- .
ship," "his holiness." what he would say
to a "peg," "something small,' a whisky
and soda, and drinks "success to the :
church and confusion to heretics! The ,
comedy of the situation makes a big
laugh, but the pathos of it make- the
collar tight. And the laugh Increases
with every false move until, in the midst I
of his trying hospitably to Persuade
champagne upon the ladies the old colonel ,
appears upon the scene and in hard, un- |
shaded fashion rebukes his lack of man
ners before them all-poor fellow, when
he meant so well. Then the laugh ceases
Lady Roydon, whose slightest wisfr is
law saves" the situation and proves her
right to a title by suddenly becoming
thirst y and asking them all to join her
in a toast, saying: "I drink to a brave
gentleman and his better acquaintance
The glasses are raised, the toast is drunk
-a pause-then once more Lieutenant
Hinds touches her hand, this time In (
gratitude, with his lips. Curtain.
Lady Roydon loves her cousin, Launce
lot*. she has told us so In look and tone, ;
and so we have the story. John s chances
of happiness are forever lost In a pressure
of the lips. In two more acts he becomes
a gentleman, of Launcelot- ma king, and
save* the boy from disgrace and discharge
by discovering in one Hutton, **Â»?,. is
Dressing for the payment of ---ambling ,
debts a thief and a blackguard who has
fobbed hi (Hinds') father of fortune and
honor, and from whom a word will send |
the fellow to penal servitude and oblivion.
Hinds has one weak moment, â¢when his
love for Lady Roydon prompts him to
withdraw his help and let Laum go o
the devil, that he may fill his place; but
his better nature quickly takes shape by
pressure from without, and he gives up,
knowing, as we all do, that it is be* so.
for, somehow, though he Is good enough.
we know that he could never make Lady
Roydon happy. "There are two classes of
men " he repeats, "gentlemen and the
rest." I fall in with the rest."
Launcelot. a worthless sort of fellow |
through false education, wins her hand
and because of love she will probably be
happy Hinds, nature's nobleman, loses
her and because of love all his happiness
is dead. So the world wags. The first act
is best in dialogue and situation, the sec
ond 'n serious interest and the third in
scene The sen. ml is too slow and made
somewhat slower than necessary by what
the elocutionist calls "too long quantity,
but it has a clever drop and win- two
call-* Hinds, Lady Roydon, Launcelot
and Hutton are four well drawn charac
ters and really make the play. After
seeing Mr. Miller as John Hinds we won

tering ' His individuality becomes a plas- ,
tic, obedient, unreasoning quality and

the most natural, and, best of all, not ex- ;
S _me a say's so many pleasant things of
Miss Anglin that the collection of adjec
tives at one's command is about ex
hausted. As Lady Roydon she is just
herself and what more can be said in
uraise? Mr. Standing gets our blessing
when he wins her, in spite of our con
viction that he doesn't deserve her, be
cause Mr. Standing digs down in the
character and shows us the spots where
its graces lie. Mr. Stevens ln the third
act wins a well-deserved scene call, and
although scene calls are bad and â¢should
not be encouraged. I clapped myself. The
Dar are generally divided, for even the #
garnishing in the way of lovers, mothers
and devoted gallants is good garnishing.
Miss Dale has one fine speech and makes
a hit with it. and Mrs Whiff en as
1 auncelot's mother, with her superfluous
and misplaced adjectives, and her pen
chant for matchmaking, is a most amus
ing figure. Mr. Allen in the small port of

novel, but it is well worked out. The

SOLDIERS IN CAMP
AND ABOUT TO SAIL
General Longstreet reviewed the Ne
braska and Pennsylvania men at the Pre
i sidio last evening at retreat. The review
took place on that, part of the old parade
I ground in front of the general hospital
! buildings and as yet uncovered by tents.
I The Nebraska men were first in line. j
They went through th.- regular evening
dress parade and then were marched past!
General Longstreet. In the meantime the j
I Pennsylvania men had held dress parade
j on the color line of the camp and they
i were marched out after the Nebraska men
! had gone. Both regiments made a fine
showing and General Longstreet express
led much pleasure at the honor that had
! been shown him. He commented very fa
vorably upon the appearance of the vol-
I unteers. -,-."â¢ â¢," : '_ _
Permission has been received from
j Washington to muster out those soldiers
' in the general hospital who are able to j
I be mustered out and who do not wish to
wait until their regiments have returned
from the Philippines. It has been the
custom and the intention to keep these 1
men until their regiments had returned
and then to muster them out with their
comrades, but the men look upon this as ;
a hardship and they wish to get out as
soon as they are able. General Shatter
was communicated with upon the sub
ject and at his request permission was
I granted by the army authorities. This
! will clear the wards of a great many who
! have been carried on the sick list await
'â¢ Ing their commands, and Golonel Girard
I will have all the more room to provide for .
! incoming sufferers. The work of discharg- j
ing these men will be commenced at once, !

1 later this week. A detachment consisting:

; Williams. G. J. Young and Harry P. Allen.

i Angel Island, has been ordered to sail j
! with her when she goes.
I Miss Rebekah Mickle and Miss Mary E. ;
Craig contract nurses, have also been or
i dered' to Manila by the same transport.
1 Leave of absence for one month has
I been granted Lieutenant Colin H. Ball,
Twentieth Kansas Volunteer Infantry.

POLITICS AT WORK IN
THE OLYMPIC CLUB
TWO NOMINATING TICKETS ARE
IN THE FIELD.
For the first time In three years politics
within the Olympic Club manifested itself
in a factional strife. At the annual elec
tion of the nominating committee last
night two tickets were placed In the Held.
Vice President McLellan presided and a
quorum of 100 members was present. The
meeting was held In the club gymnasium,
and to the surprise of the old clubmen
evolved into a political fight. There was
much discussion over the manner In which
the ticket should be voted. Some were in
favor of a composite vote on a straight
ticket others preferred a ballot for indi
viduals The former wop. out after a
lengthy debate on parliamentary laws.
The "Work of the evening began when
A M. Armstrong rose to nominate what
he termed representatives of the minor
ity He said he represented a bulk of
club' members who used the cluo daily,
and they wanted representation. Other
wise there would be discord and discon
tent. At first Armstrong was in mind to
nominate two men, but later changed to
the naming of five. Leo Pockwitz led the
opposition and he named five. Arm
strong's ticket was Frank W. Pearson,
William Cartwrlght. Al Hoffman, Roy
Welden and John Tait. Pockwitz nom
inated Tom Sawyer. Ed Zimmer. H. S.
Russ, Herman Heydenfeldt and T. C. Har
dee. Pockwitz's ticket won out handily,
as the "boss" politician had marshaled
his forces and voted them in a lump.

LUMBERMEN DISCUSS
FOREST CONDITIONS
GUILBERT PINCHOT TALKS ON
, CALIFORNIA TREES.
Meeting in the Chamber of Commerce
Brings About an Exchange of
Ideas on Forest Preserves. "
A number of prominent lumbermen and
those interested in systematic forestry as
sembled yesterday afternoon in the hall
â  of the Chamber of Commerce to discuss
: forestry conditions in the State of Cali
-1 fornia. Hugh Craig presided. Guilbert
I Pinchot, United States Commissioner of
| Forestry, was present and spoke at some
i length about the arboreal investigations
in this and other States. -:- v > '
"The lumbermen throughout the United
States ought to be interested in the con
servation of our big forests," said Mr.
Pinchot. "The private ownership of for
est lands far exceeds the national pre
serves, and the lumbermen ought to use
a wary eye for the conservation of this
I source" of supply. I suggest to you gen
j tlemen present that a thorough study of
conditions as they are in this State be
' made systematically. It would be of great
advantage to know what districts will
produce the best of certain kinds of trees
and what the commercial distribution of
trees is. It would also be wise to study
the effect of chaparral growth upon the
bigger trees and to ascertain what land
is alone fitted for timber purposes "
After Commissioner Pinchot finished his
address a general discussion was had late
in the afternoon.

COUNTY CLERK WILL
KEEP PRESENT FORCE I
DEPUTIES DEVISE THE WAYS
AND MEANS THEREFOR.
Although the Board of Supervisors
Refused the Requested Relief
the Public Will Not
Suffer.
As the Supervisors have refused to al
low County Clerk Deane a further allow
ance that official and his force of depu
ties have arranged matters so that not
withstanding the effort, on the part of
Mayor Phelan and others to cut the force
to thirty-four men the public will not
suffer. Several weeks ago, when the
Supervisors made the appropriation for
the expenses of the County Clerk's office,
an investigation was made by Mr. Deane
and it was found that it was absolutely
impossible to run the office on the
Mayor's figures. Acting Chief . Deputy
Goddard went before the board and ex
plained the situation. He showed the
board that litigation would be impeded,
estates in course of ' settlement tied up
and perhaps criminals freed If the force
of the office was reduced to inefficient
numbers.
The argument, however, had no effect
on the board and the appropriation was
allowed to stand as first fixed. In con
sequence Mr. Deane, appreciating the
damage which would result to litigants
and the public in general If the Mayor s
wishes were carried out, began to devise
ways and means by which the force or
the offlce could be kept up and the ex
penses still run within the appropriation.
In the meantime the many deputies of the
office, each knowing that the action of
the board meant that some twenty men
would be dropped from the roll if some
plan was not devised, held an informal
meeting and decided that if County Clerk
Deane would keep the present force they
would see that the amount allowed
monthly for salaries was so divided that
each man would get his share of the
whole, the Importance of the offices and
salary heretofore drawn by each man be^
ing considered in the division.
This matter arranged. County Clerk
Deane was called into consultation. Al
though regretting that the board could
not be convinced that an extra appro
priation was needed that the efficiency of
the force might be kept up, and feeling
that the division of the various salaries
was to some extent a sacrifice on the
part of many of the deputies, Mr. Deane
said that in his opinion the public good
was first to be considered, lf the men
had all agreed to the plan as stated, he
said that he had no objection, and that
their agreement had lifted from his
shoulders the further burden of devising
ways by which litigation could be con
tinued without interruption. In conse
quence the force, through the efforts of
Mr. Deane and those under him, will re
main in Its present efficient condition.
Speaking of the situation, Mr. Deane
said last evening that the action of the
board had embarrassed him to some ex
tent, but did not discourage him. He was
confident that although Mayor Phelan
and those who stood by the reduction
measure did not understand the situation
and refused him the needed relief, some
way would be devised by which the public
needs would be attended to. The action
of the County Clerk and his subordinates
caused general satisfaction among the
members of the bar, for the public has
been saved great inconvenience if not
loss, notwithstanding the adverse stand
taken by those who, behind the mask they
hold bearing the slogan "for the public
good," perform official acts to the public
damage.

KIDNAPING OF AH MOY.
The Preliminary Examination of
Wong You Commenced Before
Judge Treadwell.
The preliminary examination of Wong
You, charged with the kidnaping of
pretty Ah Moy some weeks ago from
Chinatown, was commenced before Judge
Tread well yesterday.
John J. Kerrigan, the hackman, who
was arrested along with Wong You, had
the charge against him withdrawn on the
understanding that he would be a wit
ness for the prosecution against Wong
You, -hut after he got on the stand he
showed a reluctance In telling what he
knew. He related how Wong You had
hired his hack and that he drove him and
two other Chinese and the woman to
Holy Cross Cemetery, where they left
the "hack, one of the Chinese carrying the
woman on his back.
Policeman Herve testified to arresting
Kerrigan and the defendant at Ocean
View while driving back t.i the city. The
ease was continued until Thursday. The
police have as yet been unable to dis
cover the whereabouts of Ah Moy.

ACTED IN SELF-DEFENSE.
Charge of Murder Against John P.
Wilson Dismissed by Judge
Mogan.
The case of John P. "Wilson, charged
with th. murder of Walter Cavanaugh in
a saloon on Ellis and Mason streets a
week ago, was dismissed by Judge Mogan
yesterday on the ground that Wilson act
ed in self-defense.
The Judge reviewed the evidence care
fully and said that from the evidence of
the witnesses for the prosecution it was
apparent that Cavanaugh had determined
to kill Wilson, and if Wilson had not pro
tected himself he certainly would have
been killed. Wilson did not seek Cava
naugh, but Cavanaugh sought him with
murder in his heart.

BIGAMIST OSGOOD IN COURT.
Trial of the Much-Married Man Be
gun Before Judge Mogan.
The case of Bigamist I.eander George
Osgood came up before Judge Mogan yes
terday, but the trial of the much-married
man did not progress very far.
Two of the five wives of the defendant-
Mrs. Mary* Frances Wynne, who is No. 3,
and Mrs. Tillie Alice Glover, who is No.
4â were in court, but their testimony was
not needed at this especial stage of the
case The entire afternoon session was
consumed by Prosecuting Attorney
Joachimsen In providing witnesses to
prove the legality of Osgood flrst mar
riage ten years ago, on which occasion
Miss Nellie J. Kinsella. daughter of Shoe
maker Kinsella of 719 Natoma street, of
ficiated in the unhappy role of the biga
mist's bride. There are but two living
witnesses to this weddingâ Miss Loretta
Kinsella. sister of the bride, and Father
Scanlan, who performed the ceremony.
Miss Kinsella's present whereabouts is
unknown, and Father Scanlan is away on
his vacation. â¢ â¢
The witnesses examined yesterday were
John Kirfsella. father of Mrs. Osgood No.
1: Justice Clift of Oakland, who officiated
whPn Osgood took his third wife, and Al
bert C. Parker of Stockton, who joined
Mrs. Tillie Glover to the bigamist.
Until it is proved that Osgood was le
gally united to Nellie Kinsella on, the
i"th of February. ISS9. none of the women
who have since joined hands and hearts
with him may be allowed to testify. To
obtain this necessary testimony a sub
pena was Issued for Father Scanlan and
the case went over till Wednesday at 2
o'clock.
Peter Dunne Is. looking after the Inter
ests of the man who successively led to
the altar Miss Nellie Kinsella, Miss Angle
Mills of Bakersfleld, Mrs. "Wynne and Mrs.
Glover. .

To Honor Goethe.
I Everywhere in the United States as well
j as in Germany extensive preparations are
being made to celebrate the approaching
I one hundred and fiftieth anniversary of
the birthday of the great poet Goethe in
a becoming manner. Here in San Frcn
cieco the celebration will be under the
auspices of Der Deutsche-Calirornisch
Verein and is to consist of a free literary
and musical entertainment in Metropoli
tan Hall on Monday evening, August 28.
Among the orators who are to appear
may be mentioned President Jordan and
Professor Goebel of Stanford University
and President Wheeler of the University
of -California provided the latter is able
to reach California in due time. President
Jordan will speak of "Goethe the Natural
ist" and Professor Goebel of "Goethe the
Literary Genius."
