COURT NOTES.
William T. Reed, who took a shot at
George C. Smth. a rival watchman at 722
Commercial street, recently and was
charged with assault to murder, had his
case dismissed by Judge Treadwell yes
terday, as' the complaining witness Â«liii
not care to prosecute.
The preliminary examination of Edwin
Muscat, charged with the murder of Mrs.
Sophia Sebastina Sanchez, was continued
before Judge Mogan yesterday, when Wil
liam Longan, chief machinist, B. K. M- -
Morris, ensign, and A. B. Alexander, of
the United States steamer Albatross, tes
; tified as to the good character of the de
fendant while on the vessel and that two
days before the murder he hail asked to
re-enlist. The case was continued till
August 8.
R. ***. Cavanaugh, who was arrested on
Sunday night at his home at Tev.nty-third
and York streets for heating his crippled
wife, was convicted by Judge Mogan yes-
I terday and ordered to appear for sentence
I this morning, when be will get three
; months in the County Jail.
Mrs. Minnie K. Look swore to a com
; plaint in Judge Mogan's court yesterday
for the arrest of Attorney John F. Car
! roll on a charge of misdemeanor embez-
I zlement. She alleged that she gave Car
roll $47 in December last to institute suit
ior divorce and Carroll had not even filed
the necessary papers.
When the officers of the Twinkling Star
Improvement Company, charged with
maintaining a public nuisance in the
shape of the Hotel Nymphia, appeared
i before Judge Graham yesterday their at
torney asked for time to plead, and the
I cases were continued till Friday.
T. B. Broderick, livery-stable keeper.
! 332 Bush street, swore to a complaint in
i Judge Mogan's court yesterday for the
arrest of Thomas Maguire and three
i others on the charge of malicious mis
[ chief. He said that they hired a horse
and buggy from him on Saturday, and on
1 Sunday the rig was discovered with the
horse badly used up and the buggy almost
smashed in pieces.
Sane-iorgt Hugo, an ex-member of the
I United States Cavalry, swore to a com
plaint in Judge Graham's court yesterday
for the arrest of W. P. Riokman. a
plumber on Mission street, near Sixteenth,
on a charge of obtaining money by false
pretenses. Hugo alleged that he gave
Rickman $500* for the half interest in a
saloon at 16 Mason street and Rickman
sold out and kept all the money.
Benjamin Moore, a soldier, who was
j arrested on Sunday on Kearny street by
Policeman Kavanaugh for begging, plead
ed guilty before Acting Police Judge
Kerrigan yesterday, but a corporal and
three men from the Presidio were in'
court and asked that Benjamin be turned
over to them, as he was wanted for
petty larceny at the Presidio and deser
tion. He was handcuffed and marched
out of the court, and will probably be
dishonorably discnarged from the army.
John J. Reilly, the soldier who asked for
10 cents from a newspaper man at Market
and Kearny streets on Saturday, was con
victed of begging by Judge Graham yes
terday and was sentenced to thirty days
in the County Jail.
George "Watson and George Radcliffe,
the two young men charged with assault
to rob. appeared before Judge Graham
yesterday. M. H. Kinney, the man who
was knocked down by the defendants and
two others at Sixth and Minna streets
early Sunday morning, refused to swear
to a complaint against them, and the
Judge continued th.- case till to-morrow
to see what he could do.
The case of Cornelius Manning, charged
with the murder of Mrs. Mary Kennedy,
was called in Judge Conlan's court yes
terday, but owing to the enforced ab
sence of the Judge through his boy's
death Acting Judge Kerrigan continued
it till Tuesday, August 15.
Francisca Rosales. 228 Brannan street.
swore to a complaint in Judge Graham's
court yesterday for the arrest of Jesus
Garcia on a charge of battery. She al
leged that while she was sick in bed on
Sunday Garcia came into her bedroom
and struck her several times in the face.
The charge of grand larceny against
Grace Foster was dismissed by Judge
Treadwell yesterday. She was accused
of stealing a diamond pin from Walter H.
Levy of the Mirror saloon on Kearny
street and Levy told the Judge yesterday
that he would not prosecute the case.
Mrs. John Wallis. 1402 Pacific street,
swore to a complaint in Judge Mogan's
court yesterday for the arrest of her
sister-in-law. Mrs. Helen Wallis, on a
charge of disturbing the peace. She al
leged that since her sister-in-law had been
divorced from her husband she had been
annoying her by calling at her house and
threatening to kill her because the court
had given her the custody of her child.
The will of the late William A. Piper,
heretofore published, was filed for probate
yesterday. The will was presented by
Attorneys Bishop & Wheeler, and Heller
& Powers, representing the Union Trust
Company and William Giselman, execu

Senator Sammie Braunhart has quit.
Early last evening he took a final look
at the perplexing situation, and in ring
parlance threw up the sponge and ad
mitted to several of his opposing Demo
crats that his chances at the primary
to-day would be too slim to warrant a
show of what little strength he had.
Before he visited the railroad head
quarters Sammie felt sure that he
could pull the Committee of One Hun
dred of the Democracy through in the
THE RAINEY-EXAMINER ROUND-UP.
Twenty-ninth District, but when the in
terview was over the Senator found that
the support he looked for had already
been given to the Examiner-Rainey
combination, and Sammie was told that
he must shift for himself and incident
ally the Committee of One Hundred. Be
fore leaving the office, however, he was
handed a small book, entitled, "How to
interview a railroad political boss with
out the knowledge of a newspaper."
A queer state of affairs exists in the
camp of the Committee of One Hun
dred of the Democracy in the Fortieth
District.
M. C. Hassett had his eye on the lead
ership of that district. Ed Rock in his
desire to get to. the front told his many
friends that he also could handle the
lines In good shape. When Hassett
learned of Rock's ambition he hastened
to him and tried, to explain to Rock that
he (Hassett) would better satisfy the
district as Its leader. Mr. Rock could
not see it that way, and in order to
settle the matter Rock suggested that
both call a meeting and the one show
ing the larger roll of legitimate voters
would assume the leadership. Hassett
consented to the plan and meetings
were called, and Rock, it is said, won
according to the agreement.
Hassett was not satisfied with the re
sult and repaired forthwith to head
quarters with his grievance. The Com
mittee of One Hundred tried to smooth
the matter over by coaxing Rock to
step down, but Mr. Rock was firm and
no amount of pleading could move him.
At last reports the committee had
failed to patch up the difficulty, and
both aspirants for leadership honors
have agreed to fight it out to a finish at
the polls to-day.
Rainey and the Examiner In the For
tieth are anxious to Join forces with the
Committee of One Hundred of the
Democracy represented by M. C. Has
sett in that district and are offering and
suggesting all sorts of compromises
and combinations in order to squeeze In.
Hassett does not take very kindly to 'â 
! the idea and declares that he would j
I rather have his wing of the Democratic 'â¢â 
party defeated in an honest, open fight
! than to win a few delegates and have
the stain of Examiner- Raineyism on ;
j them.
BUCKLEY HEADQUARTERS
I The Blind Man's Followers
Propose to Shape the Des
tiny of the Democracy.
All was activity in the headquarters of ;
! Buckley last night. The rooms were |
; crowded with scores of young men, over
j half of whom had the general appearance
of having worked hard during the day at
their respective trades. The topic of dis
cussion was the primary election and the I
chances of the tickets in the various As
sembly districts to-day. .
The trend of the conversation often led ;
to the part the Examiner had taken In ,
the fight and the onslaught it has made :
on Buckley and the men who support him.
It seemed to be the universal opinion |
i among tho workingmen who compose the !
I different clubs that the harsh things said j
j of Buckley's followers are intended for
! them, and they therefore are indignant.
While Buckley does not hold Phelan re- !
j sponsible for the reviling he and his \
{friend* have received in the columns of j
THE SAN, AX CISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 8, 1899.
the Examiner, his followers do. The fol
lowers of Mr. Buckley are determined to
continue their organizations whether they
be successful at the polls to-day or not.
They number many thousands and feel
they can wield some influence in shaping
the destiny of their party. .
In a general conversation Buckley re
gretted that the members of the Fire De
partment were compelled to get out and
work hard for the success of Ralney's
ticket. He said he was advised last night
that the order had gone forth to the brave
boys who risk their lives for a paltry sum
and whose bread and butter depends on
their positions to get out and help the
man who controls their very existence.
He also said that he had no ambitions to
gratify and ail he desired was that the
Democratic citizens of this city be per
mitted to go out to-day and cast their
votes without molestation.
In answer to a question put to him by
one of his friends as to whether ne was
aware of the attack on him in the Exam
iner he replied that he was familiar with
the assault. "The attacks on me," he
said, "are personal, not political. Some
years ago the then managing editor of
that paper called on me and wanted me to
nominate a man for Congress by the name
of Jerry* Lynch, a stock broker, who had
failed in business, stating that Lynch
would pay him $3000 for his aid. Of course
I refused to have anything to do with it.
"This scurrilous attack is made on me
in a general way. They do not point to
any specific transaction, but they take
their slush bucket and daub their vile
slanders all over me. I have charged
them in a specific manner with acts that
they do not dare refute. Why don't the
Examiner reply?
"The "Mudmiir speaks of association
with a corrupt boss. Max Popper is tly-Ir
intimate friend. They have hidden him
during this campaign for reasons best
known to themselves. Notwithstanding
that Popper had knowledge of all the cor
ruption done in politics they still cham
pion his cause. The Examiner like Pop
per, when called Upon to defend itself, re
fuses to answer. _'__': . Â
â¢'The programme of the managing edi
tor of the Examiner at this time is that
he wants to control the convention for the
same Ignoble purposes his predecessor
had. It looks as if he would like to sell
nominations. '"â â¢-â¢; _â¢â â *'
"I have advised my men to keep the
peace to-morrow, and I have asked tho
Chief of Police to send his officers to the
various precincts to protect these citizens.
I only ask that the citizens of this city be
given a fair chance to choose* the men
they want in the convention. The law |
provides that at the election to-moi row a
Comity Committee be elected. As the men j
who compose that body control the party, !
it behooves the citizens of this city to j
choose wisely between the people and the ,
unholy Examiner-Rainey alliance."
THANKED THE CALL. .
Members of the Thirty- j
District Compliment the-
Paper for Its Fairness.
The delegates and representatives .of
the conference committee of the Demo
cratic League of Clubs of the Thirty-first
District met last night to air their griev
ances for the treatment they had received
from the Examiner and the Bulletin. It
had been charged that the delegates rep- |
resented the boss element in Democratic I
politics and were not true to the candi- |
dacy of Mayor Phelan. All of these |
charges were denied by numerous speak
ers, and the papers making them were .
roundly condemned. The Call was com
plimented for the fairness It had shown |
to all parties during the campaign, and j
upon a rising vote It was unanimously re- j
solved that every member of the league j
should give it his support.
Ex-Assemblyman Timothy Treacy, one |
of those who had been assailed, led in the
denunciation of the league's enemies. He
said: "We are not masquerading. We are
attending to our own business and trying
to "work in the interests of the people of
thlsdlstrict. We have indorsed Phelan, and
when we say we have done that we have
gone far enough. But the bosses are mas
querading behind the good name and pop
ularity of the Mayor. From one side we
hear that if we are not taking orders from
McNab or some other boss we must be in
the camp of Buckley or Rainey. Our
crime seems to be that we are Independ
ent of them all, and for our independence
we have been assailed by the organs of
these political corruptionists, the Exam
iner and the Bulletin."
J. C. A. Wehr, Horace Jackson, Gus
Asmussen and many others spoke in a
similar strain. One of the speakers said
that their delegates had been charged
with being cutthroats, hoodlums and ev
erything else that was bad, but stated
that they represented over $100,000 worth
of taxable property in the district, by
wav of refutation.
Chairman Charles Holcomb stated that
the delegates who were receiving the. sup
port of the Examiner had not been named
by the people, but had been put up at
a private conference held downtown. He
stated further: "The Examiner has gone
Into politics and is no longer in the busi
ness of giving the news. It should be.
treated the same as any other political
â  boss. It is the duty of the people .to
stand by the paper that treats them fairly.
and prints the truth. I refer to The Call.
The mention of this paper was greeted
with cheers. -tftf.- yy- -.-"v
The meeting was a representative one
and very enthusiastic.

iner-Rainey combination has joined forces
with the Committee of One Hundred of
the Democracy. There is also an Inde

Charles Edelman a prominent resident
of Orange County.' is visiting friends in
San , Francisco. Mr. Edelman was a can
didate for the nomination for Treasurer
at the Democratic State Convention held
at Sacramento last summer. It was then
strongly urged that Orange County should
be represented on the ticket. While visit
ing here Mr. Edelman has consented to
serve as a delegate In the .Democratic
Municipal Convention of San Francisco.
The Committee of One Hundred being shy
of resident material placed Mr. Edelman
on the ticket as one of the representatives
in the Thirty-ninth District. _ â  '
The Democrats of the Thirty-first. Dis
trict have issued a circular to the voters
of that district urging them not to be de
ceived by the bosses, who claim to be
friendly to Mayor Phelan but who are in
fact not in favor of honest government.
They ask all voters desirous of a clean,
decent government to vote the ticket
headed by Charles H. Holcomb in that
district.

AFTER THE TURNTABLES.
Supervisors Called Upon to Declare
Them Public Nuisances.
The Supervisors will make another at
tempt to rid the streets of the turntables
operated by the . Market-street Railway
Company. A resolution with that point in
view was introduced in the Board of Su
pervisors yesterday by Supervisor Bying
ton. The preamble of the resolution re
cites that the Supreme Court decided
when an attempt was made to remove the
turntable at Geary and Market streets
that the matter could only be reached by
general regulation and not by special or
der. Upon" this decision Supervisor By
ington based his resolution, which makes
the construction and maintenance of any
turntable or similar device for turning or
switching cars upon public street cross
ings a misdemeanor punishable by a fine
of not less that $100 nor more than $1000,
or Imprisonment for six months, or both.
The resolution was passed' to print.
SEWER COMMISSION INVALID.
No Law Exists for Its Creation by
the ' Supervisors.
General Dickinson yesterday' advised
Auditor Wells that In his opinion the
sewer commission appointed by the Board
of Supervisors was not a legal body. The
law, he said, empowered the Board of Su
pervisors to appoint either an architect
or an engineer, but not to create a com
mission. Auditor Wells immediately noti
fied Mayor Phelan and his attorney,
Daniel C. Murphy, and together they went
to Wells' office, where a hot discussion
ensued. General Dickinson read the sec
tion which he has discovered and now the
Mayor will attempt to straighten the mat
ter out by having the board appoint one
of the present commission a chief engi
neer and empower him to employ assist
ants. If the present commission had
been permitted to proceed any bond Issue
that might have ensued for sewer pur
poses would have been invalid.

FARALLONES OUT OF THE WAY
THREE SAILING VESSELS WILL
RACE TO HONOLULU.
Plenty of Room in Hunters Point
Drydock for the Hancock â
Vessels Building Ove
rdue Ships.
The transport Morgan City was sched
uled to sail for Manila to-day with 800
recruits, but' owing to the non-arrival of
some freight she will not now get away
before Thursday. The St. Paul is. going
to Seattle to help carry the Fourth Cavalry
to Manila, the Siam will not be ready
for her cargo of horses for ten days, the
Hancock will be at least six weeks at the
Union Iron Works, the Relief will be
overhauled and will go back to the Philip
pines for another load of wounded, the
Senator is coaling at Broadway wharf and
the Leelanaw discharged her Filipinos
yesterday and went to the Union Iron
Works.
In connection with the arrival of the
I transport Sherman with the California
boys a big mistake has been made. Colo
nel Duboce has cabled that he will so ar
range matters that the transport will be
off the Faraliones at noon of the day
she will arrive. What he should have done
was to have her off Point Reyes at that
hour. The Point is the first land Captain
Grant will make on a direct course from
Yokohama to San Francisco, and in or
der to make the Faraliones he will have
to go out of his way. Directly the Sher
man is spoken off Point Reyes the fact
would be known in San Francisco, thus
giving at least two hours and a half to
make necessary preparations. From the
Faraliones the proposition is to bring the
news in by tug, but there is not a tug in
the bay that can outrun the Sherman,
and more than likely both will be sighted
together by the Merchants' Exchange
lookout at Point Lobos. It should be so
arranged that the Sherman will be report
ed from Point Reyes and then everybody
will have ample time to reach the water
front before the transport gets in.
A three-cornered race from here to
Honolulu will begin to-morrow and con
siderable Interest is attached to the event,
as the vessels are three of the fastest of
the sugar fleet. The barkentine S. N.
Castle will get away this afternoon, while
the barkentine Planter and the bark S. C.
Allen will leave together early Wednesday
morning. Captain Hubbard of the Castle
Is going to make the most of his start
and expects to get to Honolulu first even
if he does not win the race. Captain Mc-
Neill of the Planter is sure that he will
be first to sight Diamond Head and has
bet Captain Perriman a suit of clothes
that he will make the islands first. The
vessels are very evenly matched and it Is
a toss up as to which one will win.
Two new vessels for the lumber trade
and one for the oil trade are being built
at Eureka. One of the lumber vessels will
be the steamer Dispatch, for Gray &
Mitchell, and the other will be the
schooner Alexander, for Charles Nelson.
The Alexander will carry 800,000 feet of
lumber, and Captain Senate of the Allen
A will command her. The Dispatch will
have first-class passenger accommoda
tions, and will carry 650.000 feet of lumber.
The transport Hancock will dock at the
Hunters Point drydock. It was stated a
few days ago that the vessel would have
to go to Hongkong or Port Orchard to
drydock, as she was too long for Hunters
Point. This was incorrect, as there is
not only room for the Hancock there but
eighteen feet to spare. All arrangements
for docking the vessel have been made,
and as soon as she is overhauled at the
Union Iron "Works she will go on Hunters
Point. It is the intention of the California
Drydock Company, shortly, to lengthen
the Hunters Point drydock to 610 feet on
th" sill, and Howard C. Holmes, the chief
engineer of the Harbor Commission, is
engaged in getting up the plans and speci
fications. __ _._,__. '
The British ship Hyderabad is lying in
the stream, loaded and ready to sail for
England, but it will be several days be
fore she will get away, as Captain Scott
is lying aboard dangerously ill.
The British ship Macduff is now out ISO
days from Calcutta for Astoria, and 30
per cent reinsurance is being paid on her.
If she does not arrive soon her grain bags
will be of no use for this season's crop.
The President Thiers, now out 229 days
from San Francisco for Liverpool, is be
ing reinsured at 80 per cent, and the Cas
well 171 days out from Newcastle, N. fc>.
V\" for Guayaquil; at 90 per cent.
The Mall company's steamer San Jose
arrived from Panama and way ports yes
terday. She brought up a large New
York cargo and sixteen cabin and ten
steerage passengers besides six Chinese
in transit to Hongkong. "..*
The tug Rescue arrived from San Diego
with the schooner Sequoia in tow*. The
Sequoia was on fire, and has been brougnt

WANTED TO HUG THEM.
An Insane Young Man Makes Violent
Love to Two Girls.
A young man giving his name as George
Marks yesterday afternoon accosted two
girls on Sutter street, near Scott, and
attempted to hug them. Their screams
attracted the attention of a citizen, who
notified the sergeant at the O'Farrell
street station. An officer was hurriedly
dispatched to the scene, and he placed
Marks under arrest. As he seemed to be
mentally unbalanced he was taken to the
Receiving Hospital and locked up in the
Insane ward pending an examination as
to his sanity. ___
Urge Early Closing of Stores.
This evening a mass meeting of the Vigi
lant Society will be held at Golden Gate
Hall, 625 Sutter street, for the purpose of
urging all retail dealers to close their
stores at 6 o'clock on Saturday evening.
The proposition is advanced for the bene
fit of women and children employes. The
meeting will be addressed by Mrs. John
Pettee, president of the Vigilant Society,
and other prominent citizens. All employ
ers and employes of retail stores are re
quested- to be present. Addresses. will be
Interspersed by the following musical pro
gramme: Vocal selections. Miss Millie
Flynn: vocal solo. Miss Maud Pettee; ac
companist. Miss May Pettee;. selections,
Columbia quartet; grand concert of fifty
pieces, Professor Graeber's Banjo Club.

HARRY COOPER THINKS
HE WAS A GOOD THING
GUARANTEED A $12 BELL AND
IS SUED FOR $1200.
Claims He Was Fooled by David
Bloom, the Artist, and Phil
Montague.
Harry R. Cooper of Cooper's bookstore,
746 Market street, thinks that he has been
made the victim of misplaced confidence.
His establishment was attached 0:1 Sat
urday afternoon in a suit for $1200 filed
by the Sunset Photo-engraving Company.
The matter involves the publication of
the history of thÂ© First California Regi
ment, with which Cooper says he had
nothing to do except to befriend the pub
lishers. David Bloom, the artist, and Phil
Montague, ex-secretary of the Press Club.
According to Mr. Cooper's story Mon
tague rushed into his place some months
ago and told him that the Sunset people
had $12 worth of cuts for the book which
they refused to surrender without the cash
or a guarantee that it would be paid.
Cooper wrote a note to the firm .hat he
would stand good for whatever Montague
had there. No amount was specified, and
right there is where Cooper thinks he
made a blunder that promises to be ex
pensive. On the strength of his note the
photo-engravers turned out $1200 worth of
work for Bloom & Montague. When the
bill was presented to Cooper and his at
tention called to the guarantee he was
dumfounded and refused payment. Hence
the suit. â Â _.
"If I owed the money, said Mr Cooper,
"I would pay it in a minute, but it was
my understanding that I was only guar
anteeing $12. The matter will be fougnt
in the courts."
The attachment was released yesterday
morning.
CITY HOSPITAL LOTS.
According to Old Laws They Belong
to San Francisco.
The city of San Francisco has won In
its actions against Honora Sharp, Eliza
M. Sharp and John H. Dunham. The ac
tions were to quiet title to a tract of land
which was designated on the Van Ness
map of 1856 as "hospital lots," and which
was originally part of the pueblo lands
of the city within the corporate lands as
defined in ISSI. The land was included in
the scope of the Van Ness and other or
dinances relating to the disposition of
pueblo lands, and which wer.- ratified by
the Legislature of March. 1853. â 
Defendants had possession of a portion
of the tract, and the possession so held
by them was derived continuously through
their predecessors in interest since i time
prior to January 1, 1855. The court below
rendered judgment declaring that the city
is the owner of the land in suit "in trust
for the use and benefit of the people of
the State of California and the inhabitants
of the city and county of San Francisco
for the purposes of a public hospital," and
that the defendants have no rights or in
terest in any part of the same.
ATTEMPTED SUICIDE.
A Despondent Restaurant Keeper
Turns on the Gas.
Because his wife took apartments on
another floor of his lodging house H. Le
Blanc, proprietor of the Saddle Rock res
taurant, 115 Stockton street, attempted to
end his life early yesterday morning in
his sleeping room. The despondent caterer
turned on the gas on retiring shortly after
midnight, and had it not been for the vigi
lance of the night clerk, who knew* that
Le Blanc and his wife were not on speak
ing terms, the would-be suicide would
doubtless have gone to the borne he
sought. '-.".- _/â  , __. Â
During the day Le Blanc and his wife
quarreled. Mrs. Le Blanc proved her hus
band's master in the wordy war which fol
lowed, and to even matters Le Blanc spent
an hour or two cutting her clothes to
shreds. ! Mrs. Le Blanc occupied a room in
another part of the house to annoy her
husband. * . -^ . â _â¢"â _"
When the night clerk, who smeller" the
escaping gas. broke Into Le Blanc room
he found both jets turned full on. Le
Blanc resented this visit as an intrusion
and attempted to claim that the night
clerk and special officer turned on the gas
when they entered the room. At latest
accounts he was still alive and kicking.
